RATING: 8
At a high level, Notre Dame reminds me of Puerto Rico - the same sort of richness of ideas which all mesh together with seemingly lots of options and therefore strategies to pursue. The ideas are different ones to Puerto Rico but they evoked the same feel. The game seems to scale well from two - five and certainly should provide the sort of replayability that satisfies me (10-20 games). beyond that then it may well turn out to be too scripted - just as Puerto Rico can

RATING: 8.5
Placement game with multiple paths to victory.

Appears to scale well, has a relatively fresh card-drafting mechanism (actually very similar to Citadels), and appears to have multiple possibilities/strategies to score victory points and thereby win.  My main issue is that I was expecting a real gamer's game, and with the significant dose of luck via the card-drawing/passing, this can only be regarded as a solid middleweight.

That said, it is an enjoyable game, and a very solid design, and it was nice to see a game finally published with proper sized cards.

Own the Treasure Box [boardgame=165350]New Persons[/boardgame] expansion cards.

RATING: 7
This is a good game and one I'd play most often that it was suggested.  On the positive side, there's  a lot of good decision making to be done, it's quick and maintains your interest throughout.  In middling terms, the theme whilst a bit abstract is just about there and is certainly better than some abstractish Euros I've played.  On the negative-side a fellow gamer remarked that it hadn't got much player interaction, and I agree with that.  I like that in a game a lot, but if another doesn't then he might well be looking at scoring this as an 8 or 9 here.  There is the card passing and the ability to use the coach to nab chits before other players can, so there is at least some inter-action.

Funwise I'd give this 7 out of 10 and so I think that's where I'm going to place it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
*Rating based on one play.

I first played this in 2013, and it is a game that shows its age.  It does not have the same feeling of depth and intricacy of recent Feld designs like Bora Bora and Castles of Burgundy.  The card drafting mechanic in this game is uninteresting and problematic.  It is problematic because you are always passing your cards to the same player, as opposed to a game like 7 Wonders where you switch directions in passing cards.  This puts players sitting next to inexperienced players at an advantage.  Additionally, the small hand size and limited usefulness of the cards make the drafting decisions quite simple and easy.

On top of that, the rat mechanic that is a rather large part of the game feels like it is not well balanced.  Granted I have only played once, but in that game, I was able to ignore my rat population for most of the game and I won handily.  I only bothered to deal with the rats when it was easy to do so; otherwise, I simply let the rats pile up and took the penalty, which does not seem steep enough to warrant the investment it takes to keep rat populations down.

All in all, my experience with Notre Dame was a disappointing one.  It's not a bad game by any stretch, it is just nowhere near the top of my favorite Feld list.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Abstract:
A card-driven euro game by Stefan Feld. Each player has a deck of cards and a district board that is connected with the other players' board. All players draw cards, then activate them to put influence cubes on their districts and activate the particular action (the more influence cubes, the more powerful). Each player also has a carriage which can be move to collect bonus tiles.

Round overview:
a) reveal new person cards (can be bribed for special abilities)
b) choose action cards (draw three, keep one, pass over remaining cards to next player)
c) play action cards (activate action of a province by placing influence cube)
d) bribe one person (get one-time ability for one coin)
e) determine plague value (subtract modifiers, adjust rat tracks)

Pros:
+ Indirect player interaction by card drafting, majority in cathedral, and collecting embassy tiles with carriage
+ Person cards add dynamic: move influence cubes, get boni for different conditions 
+ Tricky influence management: improve efficiency by placement and shifting cubes
+ Carriage movement: get tiles before the other players
+ Feels balanced in all aspects
+ Different ways to play
+ Plays well with all player counts (2-player has a different taste because one of the two drafted cards comes back)
+ Quick play-time for such kind of game
+ Nearly family-friendly (gateway plus game)
+ Easy to learn (9 action cards and 3 Persons, after the third round, everyone knows most of the game)
+ Quick turns, smooth game flow

Neutrals:
# Disease/rat management: push your luck and optimization, however, some people do not like being punished directly
# Theme is okay
# Some randomness and luck: drawn cards for actions and persons, number of rats
# A small memory element: which cards have been played
# Nearly no hidden information (except for cards drawn for drafting)
# No take-that elements
# Geometry of the player boards is unique
# Rather tactical than strategical
# Might be a bit too light for some gamers
# Sometimes a revealed person card may favor only one particular player (especially the cards from the expansion), however, all persons in play should be published at set-up time so that players have the chance to adjust their strategy
# Scoring happens during the round, between rounds, and at the end of the game

Cons:
- Component quality is okay but very thin tokens and player boards
- Component quality has not been improved in the 10th Anniversary edition (at least: it already includes the must-have expansion "The New Persons" which increases variability and replayability)
- Iconography is good for the most part but not always intuitive
- No player aids

Thoughts:
Notre Dame has just the right amount of complexity and options to appeal to a lot of people. I like its elegance most. Everything in Notre Dame feels balanced: card drafting, bribing persons, moving the carriage, and distributing influence cubes to improve actions. It is a lighter game by Stefan Feld and some folks might wish for more complexity.

Similar to:
* 7 Wonders (a more complex card drafting game with a lot of different cards for set collection and engine building, but has no central board or carriage movement)

Player: 2-5 | Time: 45-75m

Running out of money or allowing the rats to start reducing your population and VPs is not a good idea. Very enjoyable game with a number of ways to win.

RATING: 7
Very elegant game! Laid-back and easy-going, very enjoyable. Great as a 2-player game! I love the instructional icons on everything - cards, buildings, etc - no need to constantly refer to rulebook. 2015 UPDATE: After reading over reviews and because of my decluttering kick, this one is now in the maybe pile for trading - TBD. The problem I have with it is that it seems dated in terms of artwork and game play - just another cube pushing, resource building, VP gathering game. I've since decided to reduce the numbers of these types of game for more thematic one. As such, Keythedral is one of my choice games in the old school dry-and-dull Euro category and, given my limited opportunities to play, I will likely always pick that one first. I want to focus my game collection on easier to pick-up-and-play games with minimal rules reading. Apparently, if you are familiar with the higher VP scoring cards that show up in late game, you have a leg up on your opponents as you build up your engine in the early game. This is not a good mechanic for something I might play once or twice a year.

RATING: 8
Stefan Feld adapts card drafting into a an action selection game that allows for quite a bit of strategy with minimal components. The art is well done and the game play is easy to work your way through once you have a game or two under your belt. It scales well for 2-5 thanks to a modular game board that shrinks or grows with the number of players. While as essentially theme-less as many of his games it is still a strategic game with ease of play and very good quality components. A hidden gem of this genius game designer.

RATING: 7
Even today (2015), Notre Dame stands on its own. Overall, this is one of the lighter Feld games, but it has a healthy amount of tactical and strategic decisions. Furthermore, money/resources are tight in this game and the plague threat always present; as a result, the game has a healthy amount of tension. 

If I had to criticize the game, beyond the thin cardboard chits, I would say that luck-of-the-draw is quite influential; The card-drafting mechanic eliminates some of that... but players tend to focus their actions in certain areas and having certain cards come up, with certain round cards, can be significantly helpful or harmful. But still, the game plays quickly and is quite engaging. Highly recommend for most euro gamers.

RATING: 9
My first impression is positive. The game is really a tight resource management exercise, but a great one, full of those excellent agonizing decisions I love in the greatest games. The rating is likely to go up with more plays.

UPDATE: My favorite moments in gaming all involve having to take an agonizing decision amongst just a few options. If the number of options increase too much, it becomes a calculation exercise; but with just a few options, the decision must be tense to be interesting. How can I go wrong with a game composed only of such decisions? Each decision is meaningful; yet it's not necessary to become a human computer to calculate the outcome. Up to a 10.

RATING: 10
While there's been a glut of worker placement designs in 2006-2007, I think Notre Dame does it best with the added novelty of the card drafting mechanic and a manageable play time. It's a very clever game design with multiple resources: workers, money, VPs and rats (the anti-resource) and multiple strategic paths. Perfect balance of tactical and strategic choices as one struggles between specializing for greater net gain and diversifying out of necessity. The card drafting and the rotation through the personality cards adds a nice level of randomness which should keep things fresh. Definitely a must-own game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Notre Dame is a classic Euro, but it has its own shenanigans and feels quite unique. Basically there's various areas of "expertise" a player can build to gain incremental advantages. The chance to build one or another is influenced by the cards drafted in various three-card mini-drafts at the beginning of each of the nine rounds: so the game is more about tactics than strategy. Probably the sweet spot for the game is with three players, but four could be enjoyable as well.
The main strength of the game lies in its pace: it is fast (can be played in under an hour) but gives many chances to make decisions. Definitely a good game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
I was interested in playing this game to compare it to Feld's In the Year of the Dragon.  Notre Dame wasn't even close to achieving the same level of gameplay as Dragon, but I did enjoy my first experience with 4 players.

Notre Dame was pretty random in regards to the people up for bribes and available actions for each round. RATING: 8
6/17/07 - About 40 games on BSW and a few face-to-face.  This is quickly becoming my favourite Alea game out there as I am constantly looking forward to my next game.   This game is mostly a solitaire struggle between players, though through pasing cards and stealing messages you can affect how the other players play, especially the player to your left.   You need to balance you resource and gold production while limiting the effects of the plague to earn Prestige Points along the way.  Lots to know, but once you play it is quite easy to grasp.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
Lots of familiar Feld elements such as lots of different ways to score points, something that beats you down if you dont do something about it but not so much you  cannot ignore a bit, mish mash of mechanisms with fresh elements and a good mix of tactical and strategy..  This was an early game to use the sushi go/ 7 wonders style of drafting and it uses it with action selection and a really interesting cube management mechanism where the more you play an action the more powerful that action is.... as long as you have the action cubes to scale up actions.  Accessible in rules but there is a real feeling of "is this working" whereas with other Feld games you can be much more "I can do x for 5 points or y for 6".  The base game could do with a bit more variety baked in as it is the same cards that come out all the time.  This is "fixed" to a point if you have the 10th anniversary edition but my own problem is that you then need to remember which cards are in the mix, an extra set of cards so you know which ones are being used would help to take the memory element out of the game.

RATING: 8
Alkuinnostuksen jälkeen jäänyt hieman vähemmälle pelailu, lähinnä ehkä uusien pelien myötä, sillä hyvät fiilarit kyllä ainakin itselläni on. Tunnin peliksi aika monipuolinen, on paljon eri systeemeitä, joista pitää huolehtia. Tuuria on tietysti aika paljon mukana, joten pelissä on melko paljon tilanteisiin mukautumista, eli tehdään vain se mitä sattuu pystymään. Toisaalta on olemassa erilaisia taktiikoita, joita voi yrittää toteuttaa, joten pohdittavaakin riittää. Hyvä kokonaisuus siis. (17.3.2009)

Noni. Tätä on sitte paiskittu lähinnä BSW:n suosiollisella avustuksella paljon. Onhan peli aikalailla erilainen, kun sitä jo ymmärtää melko hyvin. Ei todellakaan läisintää, ja "tuurinkin" vaikutus hieman muuttuu, tai siis tuuriansa voi niin sanotusti ohjailla. Taitoa, pelin lukua, taktikointia ja tilanteisiin reagoimista tarvitaan, jotta saa hyvät pisteet. Huippupisteet vaativat sitten jo vähän tuuriakin, tai ainakin kavereiden suosiollista avustusta. RATING: 6
[url=http://ballearicus.blogspot.com/2012/01/notre-dame.html]Notre Dame[/url] es un juego de apariencia engañosa. Es necesario tener un cariño especial a los euros y haber jugado a muchos diferentes para ver mas allá de un mueve cubos sin alma. En un juego que cumple a raja tabla los cánones del eurogame al uso, jugarlo y ser capaz de saborear sus pequeñas sutilezas es gratificante. Es en ese momento cuando descubres que estás ante un juego muy bien diseñado y equilibrado, del encaje de bolillos de sus mecánicas. De duración ajustada y un factor azaroso bajo, lo hace apto solo para jugadores que gusten de saborear de la pureza en los euros. 

Si has estado evitando Notre Dame pensando que "sólo es otro eurogame" te recomiendo cuanto menos darle una oportunidad. Sorprende gratamente.

Si te gustan los eurogames debes considerar seriamente la opción de Notre Dame.

RATING: 8.75
I really enjoy this game--way more than I expected to--and I continue to enjoy it, even after playing it many times within the course of just a few weeks. The gameplay is quick, so there's no sitting around and waiting. The only part that can induce some AP is the choosing of cards, but you only have three to choose from, followed by two to choose from, so even for a slow player, it won't take more than a minute. I've played with 2, 3, 4, and 5 players, and it works well with all, though more than 2 is better because I find it more intriguing to have no clue what third card will be passed to you (with 2 players, you get back one of the cards you just passed). I wasn't that crazy about 5, either, but that just might be because a couple of people were new to the game and took a long time to do anything (you can totally decide what you're going to do on your turn while everyone else is going, but they weren't doing that). Also, I had just played Caylus for the first time and might not have been as into the game as I could have been. :-) There are many different paths to getting VPs (the winner of my first game had tons of money and kept going to Notre Dame and also got many of the secret tokens; someone else had tons of cubes and filled up many spots; I had no money and very few cubes, but I had two cubes in the park and kept invoking the VP space to gradually build up points--I tied with the second player). It feels like a lighter game, but has many satisfying decisions to make, and it's a lot of fun. Because the card shuffle and other players' choices on what cards to pass you are different every time, you can't always employ the same strategy, which increases the playability--every game is different. The fact that it's short helps, too. If I don't have time for a long game but want something more than a mindless filler, this is perfect.

RATING: 5
I can't tell you which is more disappointing, Augsburg or Notre Dame.  The former had some ingenuity but obviously could have used some more development.  Notre Dame hangs together far better but it is such a depressing retread of past German games.  The card drafting system obviously borrows from Fairy Tale, yet none of the subtlety persists because Notre Dame mostly abrogates the need to keep certain cards from you opponents.  I find it disturbing the recent trend of 
limited player interaction games and Notre Dame is just another addition to this bloated category.  My favorite games almost all hinge upon varied and copious player interaction; mostly because such things create longevity in a game. 
 
Combine this defect with a deficient theme and an uninspired optimization game revolving around cube exchange and you get something thats banality severely detracts from its many good qualities.  I think my complaint is best summed up by a conversation with my sister who notoriously hates boardgames, yet mysteriously liked Notre Dame.  She said it was "easy" and when asked to elucidate she stated that she could focus on her game and didn't have to worry about other players impinging on her plans.  To my gaming sensibilities that sounds less like a game and more like an activity. 
 
I have to wonder if this recent downturn in quality from Alea results from a poor choice of designers.  The Golden Age of Alea games is replete with names like Tueber, Knizia, Kramer, Dorn and heck I will put Seyfarth in there too.  Almost all the mediocre entries in the line have unreliable names attached.  Frankly, I don't know how Stefan Feld has done 3 of the latest 4 Alea games when his portfolio suggests a lack of the needed spark to design a great game.

RATING: 4
Played one time using a proto-type of the game. Special thanks to BGG'er Valerie Putnam for her excellent explanation of the rules, which are all in German. Players compete for points by collecting messages and investing in certain institutions/buildings. All the while, players must control the rat population in their quarter of the city lest they flourish and eat away their investitures (sic?). This is done with decks of action cards, which players draw from and pass draws along to others to give them their range of actions for a turn. Players also bribe officials to allow themselves special actions to earn points and assist in controlling the rats. A careful balance must be maintained in collecting points and the thankless task of controlling the rats. If you are too zealous in one activity or the other, it will probably cost you the game.

RATING: 7
Clean play, with 3 quick actions per turn, with usually clear choices, yet enough depth to provide interesting decisions. A nice tension develops in deciding with the few cards dealt which actions to take - and which to pass to the opponents. Players need to focus on good hand management in order to keep up the supply of influence markers and gold, both of which are needed for taking necessary actions to gain prestige points. In close games, the rat penalty could make the difference as 2 points are lost per turn for not covering plague. UPDATE: the expansion cards seem to be a decent addition - played with them only once.

RATING: 8.5
Alea, Alea... so beautiful, so cold.  Every game thus far in the big box series has been wonderful, and this is no exception.  Stefan Feld is definitely the MVP of that series. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Another interesting, fun, light but challenging Feld game. The actions are very simple, and the game is very easy to learn: draft cards, play cards, hire people, repeat. The choices, however, are complex, and you have to keep track of not only what other players are doing, but also what cards have already been played. If you see no one is interested in parks, maybe you should go for parks. If no one seems to be keeping their bank cards, go for the banks. 

Of course, then there are the rats. Luckily, like many of Feld's games (Trajan, Macao), the rats are punishing but no too punishing, and a good player can win even after a couple of turns of being overrun. The rats just present another tough choice, between reducing your rat population or going for a bunch of points (often the points you earn in a turn will outweigh the losses you accrue from rats).

RATING: 8
UPDATE: Playing this game again recently has reminded me that it is pretty good, and I might have sold it short when I first played it. The theming is weak, but it just plays so quickly and so tightly with decent tensions, it's good fun.

I upped the rating to 8 to reflect that I think the game is great with 3 players. With 4 or 5 I think there is too much other, better stuff out there, and so maybe gets a 6 (In the Year of the Dragon hits many of the same notes for me, but has more interesting direct player interaction, and seems much better balanced, so I'd rather play that game if I had more players), but Notre Dame is very solid with 3.
---
While some early adopters have seen Notre Dame as a return to form for alea, for me I think it might be the final real evidence that the magic is gone. Notre Dame has some nice pieces to it, but it's also seriously lacking in player interaction, is a bit chaotic for its own good, and is lacking in theme. None of these things are crippling - I give it a 7 - but it also lacks anything that stands out.

To me, Notre Dame seems to exemplify the Caylus Syndrome - games that are too focussed on balancing many variables in interesting ways and not focussed enough on actually making a fun game. This multi-factorial balancing act has been done, twice actually, to good effect in Princes of Florence and Puerto Rico, both of which had good themes and some player interaction, and so they worked although they aren't among my favorites. Notre Dame is just dry. It's a fun kind of a dry, I suppose, and it does present you with agonizing choices from time to time on which card to pass, and it also plays quickly and in good time with a very brisk pace. All good. But this is still a weak entry in the alea big-box series. It seems harsh to judge it as the weakest so far (Mammoth Hunters was too long, which can be a fairly serious problem in some cases, and Fifth Avenue was great but fragile in that if players just didn't get it, it could crash and burn), but it might be.
---
Funny story: we played Monopoly at a recent game gathering (I had wanted to play it again for various reasons). It worked out pretty well. I then went on to play Tigris & Euphrates, the new edition, while Kim moved over to a game of Notre Dame.

When asked afterwards how it was, she said she liked some things, didn't like others, and generally equivocated. When asked the more straightforward question, "was it better then Monopoly?", it got a clear "no".

But the Monopoly game was pretty fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[05/04/2007] Resources are scarce and numerous trade-offs present themselves throughout the game. Cubes and money oil your economy's machine,  while rats present a threat to your neighbourhood's prosperity. This sort of economic challenge the rats pose may also be found in say Müll&Money (pollution) and Through the Ages (happiness, corruption, food consumption). These negative factors need to be kept in check. The fact that you're preoccupied to a considerable extent with fighting the game, means that player interaction tends to be relatively low (but then that's pretty common in German games). That economic motor may well stutter and suffer setbacks if you haven't planned ahead properly and failed to make sound judgements on which action cards to hold and which to pass on. This card phase features most of the player interaction found in this game, which happens to be of an indirect nature similar to Puerto Rico.As with any good economic management game, there do seem to be multiple paths to victory and choices don't appear too obvious. A very solid and well-crafted design.

RATING: 7
Notre Dame is one of those super fillers for gamers... takes about 30 to 45 minutes to play, and offers tough choices in a short time span. The heart of Notre Dame is the drafting mechanic... and this is not friendly 7 Wonders drafting, as I find myself more consciously "hate drafting" to deny my neighbors cards they need.

Notre Dame takes a few plays to learn the balance between fending off the rats and going for point grabs. Familiarity also helps you leverage the people you hire for special perks. I've played this a handful of times as a two player game, but I think this is really best with 3+ players.

My only knock is that the components are average... on par with Puerto Rico and the other Alea games. 

Update: Well, with Notre Dame out of print, I sold this game in an auction. It's a solid game and one I enjoy, but I don't need to own it. My wife didn't like it enough as a two player game, and I'm trying to keep a manageable collection of games that I really want to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
UPDATE: After a few more plays, this title has lost it's luster.  Not sure why exactly, but suspect it is a bit too much multi-player solitaire.

A few mechanisms come to mind.  You have a deck of 9 cards, you draw three, keep one, and everyone passes their remaining two cards to the player on their left.  Everyone keeps one of this passed pair, and passes the remaining card to their left.  Thus you start a round with three cards, one of which is from your stack, one is from the stack of your neighbor to the right and a third is from the player on his right.  During the round you play two of the three cards, discarding the third without revealing it.

Two interesting side notes - none of the cards is "bad" thus you always have a deliciously difficult decision in what to keep and what to pass.

Secondly,  the way the people to your right are playing effects your possible strategies.  Example, last night John and Matt were moving their carriages like nuts, I almost never did - in part because the only carriage card I ever saw was the one in my deck . . . and it wasn't always the one of my three cards I most desired to keep.


During a turn, you place influence markers, doing so immediately produces a result, while at the same time increasing your influence (and therefore the result you get) in that area of your board.  Thus there is the aspect of an area control game as well as an action-activation game, without making you think - oh this is like El Grande.

And you don't "just have cubes" you have gold to manage as well - i.e. you have two resources (plus others perhaps) to manage simultaneously.

Influence in Notre Dame costs gold in addition to influence, immediately produces victory points, and at the end of a round gives those with influence in Notre Dame victory points - per cube equal to a sent number divided by the total number of cubes in the Notre Dame pool.  i.e. more cubes from different players lowers the prestige scored for any single cubes, more cubes.  But think about this interaction.  Say I put a cube on ND early, then a second cube . . . now anyone not in the pool has to say . . . damn, how much is ND worth to me since my cube will only get me 1/3 of the VPs available.

Then you have the whole rat thing - at the end of each round you can lay for the services of one of three "artisans" I'll call them.  Characters that help you.   In turn order you choose one of them, and pay one gold for their help.  They are not exclusive to you, the other players can enlist their services as well.

On the bottom of these three character cards is a number of rats, after the artisan round, each player increases the number of rats in the Cartier* by this number minus the influence they have in their Quarter's hospital.  If you accumulate more than 9 rats, you suffer VP and influence loss.  It turns out, avoidance of the rat plague can be a big issue - and another interesting mechanism.

Finally, the game has a lot of paths to victory, you're often faced with several good ways to proceed, and the delicious choice between them.

It might suffer from players with AP, but not as much as some games as the analysis required isn't monumental.

The tao of gaming review here on BGG covers the game mechanics well.

All in all I found it a refreshing game in a market that I feel is awash with a drove of "convert your cubes to gold" clones.

Is it the best game ever?  No, but I'm not sure I can think of a game I've enjoyed the first play of more that was produced in the past two years.


*what I started referring to my board or "Quarter" to . . .

RATING: 3
There's surprising subtlety here but the game itself lacks both charm and humanity and almost deserves the multiplayer solitaire epithet. It reminds me most of a little clockwork puzzle: cute, clever, operational and pointless.  The only significant player interaction comes from the card passing (which is very dilute and uninteresting) and the very minor race game of the buggies for market chips.  The rest of the game is a relatively straightforward if internally complexly timed efficiency race driven by the weak interactions of the card passing.  

The game itself falls into two phases: 1) Getting cubes onto the board for profit, and 2) Moving cubes already on the board about profitably.  Both are efficiency games, controlled variously by the resource limits of rats, money, cubes and the card draft.  Opportunism rules.  The buildings provide a variety of ways to self-reinforcingly specialise  That pattern partly drives the card passing decisions while simultaneously devalueing it: there is too much advantage to being out of phase with the neighbour on your right to make the card passing truly interesting.

RATING: 4
Straight up Euro style resource management game but without the usual money-now-points-later type of engine building aspect to it.

It's main interest is due to the positive feedback loops in many of the choices (the more you do something, the bigger the pay-off for doing it). So, for me, this game is a classic case of find a path or resource that can pay-off for you and abuse the heck out of it while doing just enough to make sure that you don't lose too many points for neglecting other things. 

The randomness in the game is interesting and appropriate for this sort of game. The card passing aspect feel familiar because of other games like hearts. 

I sense the theme and recognize how the theme and the mechanics fit but the theme isn't exciting to me. Typically, if a game's mechanics successfully engage me, then I don't care as much about the theme (it's that way for me with Caylus - love the game, don't care about the theme). However, when the mechanics don't "wow" me then I need more from the theme and Notre Dame doesn't offer that to me. This isn't a poor reflection on the game. It's simply a reflection of my tastes.

RATING: 7
What a weird game to rate, I wanted to give it an 8, then went to 7. It's a medium weight game like Ra that plays extremely fast. It's not something that is going to be worth studying (I hope...) or something that will be the main event on any evening, but it'll be a game that you'll pull out at the start or end of a session, run through, and enjoy. It's only an hour with 5 people who know it, and I imagine the 3 player game could be cranked out in 30-45 minutes, easily.

What is this game about? Simply put: 9 actions. You have a deck with 1 of each of those actions. Each round you draw 3 cards at random from the deck (though each 3 rounds you get to all 9 cards, 9 rounds total), keep 1, pass 2 to the left. Pick 1 from the pile your right hand neighbour left behind, give the left guy the last card from that pile, and then pick up the last card form the right, which was originally part of person 2 seats away's hand. You then execute the actions. This is the same short Magic draft system that Fairy Tale used a couple of years ago, and it works here very well.

The core of the actions are all dominated by an El Grande court vs. provinces cube situation. Anytime you take an action, you drop a cube from your court into that area. As cubes build up, you aren't fighting for majorities, rather, you are simply trying to specialize. Put 1 guy in the bank, you get 1 buck. Put another there, you get 2 bucks, and so forth. Basic actions let you do that to get money, VPs, more guys from the provinces to the court, and so on. There are also ND specific ideas such as a rat menace (which some placements can hinder or eliminate) and bonus points available for going to the cathedral and tithing. The carriage is the final weird area, and is the only player interaction outside of the "everything in this game centres on it" draft, as the carriage lets you move around the board and pick up chits, some of which are on the other player's little sub-boards. At the end of each round, money is used to execute special actions. After each 3 rounds, the cathedral square in the centre of the board hands out VPs from a zero sum pool, so if too many people are there, each person will end up with less points per piece placed.

Now, this game fits together exceptionally well, and I think is is actually original, which is really saying something in this era. Specialization is clearly the right road to take in the abstract, as having a bunch of guys built up in any given area is very worthwhile. Being able to get 4$ per action, or a trio of guys, or a metric boatload of points is far superior to being able to do everything simply ok. The rub is that your ability to do most actions depends heavily on what people are passing you though, and in experienced groups, having a very powerful pair of actions means that you are going to have a hard time convincing people to pass the cards you want.

Like Magic draft, you want to not pick what the other people are taking. So, your first picks are huge, you are effectively sending signals to the player on your left about what you're willing to let through, and this forces them down certain avenues. Also like Magic draft, and as alluded to above, after a certain point, you have to cut them off, because certain abilities become ridiculous given enough specialization. In that regard, I think this is a game like Puerto Rico, where there are going to be tonnes of complaints about the weak player passing good stuff too much, so be prepared.

The scaling should offer some interesting options (particularly the 3 scale, where everyone is passing to everyone else, I would be interested in playing with 3 with Magic draft rules (i.e.: rounds 1-3: pass clockwise, 4-6 counter, 7-9 clock) as well.

Strategically, I think there is a lot of wiggle room...most of the actions are potentially good if they're used in the right way, at the right time. There are some you don't want a load of cubes on, but I think all of them are situationally good, and many of them are ungodly powerful if you can load them up with big number of people (i.e.; VP farm, Cube farm early, Bank). The Park seems like it will become better as people are more experienced, as it is less effective than the VP farm in the abstract, but it is also much more difficult to thwart, as there are a lot of ways to generate trophy bum bonus points.

Side note: Love the theme on this one, or at least the way we mangled it. Some of the mechanics are incredibly amusing if you're twisted enough in your approach to them. The Hospital creating a rat vortex for one, or the entertaining park, where pieces build up which destroy rats and then can provide extra VPs...the thematic idea? The pieces are bums. They eat the rats when they show up. They generate VPs because being able to afford having them in your district conveys a certain amount of prestige (i.e.: conspicuous consumption for that era, having bums makes you one of the "in" kids). Hence, we refer to them as trophy bums throughout the game. This sort of stuff makes the game fantastic in a ridiculousity factor 10 sort of way, and makes me enjoy it more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Reading the rules and explaining them is always a lot easier than I envisage. There is a rythm to them which makes learning them easier, even more so once you begin to learn what the symbols on the game material mean. I could even point out a mistake in the original German edition: when bribing the Bishop, you are allowed to take an action of the quarter you move the cube too; this is not mentioned in the rules. However, some of the pictograms are not very clear, but you can't have everything.

The game itself is a fairly standard but well-executed excercise in 'multiple ways to victory'. (But see below.) Players must juggle various actions and conflicting goals in order to obtain as much victory points as possible; according to the rule book this indicates proper taking care of a district of medieval Paris. As far as I'm concerned, that's a load of thematic crap: the only thing which ties the game to Paris is the Notre Dame, and even that building just represents an action amongst many. The game could have been easily called 'Kölner Dom' or 'Westminster Abbey' instead. Oh, and there's a hunchback. Okay, two items which tie the game to Paris then.

Contrary to popular opinion, I am of the opinion that player interaction in this game is [u]minimal[/u]. You see your opponents on three occasions: card swapping, the Notre Dame, and the carriages. The card swapping has you sending off two cards to your neighbours, but since you have little control over what you get, in most cases you keep the card which is the best for your own position. When another player is 'obviously' persuing a certain strategy, then yes, your choices will be influenced by it. Otherwise, you simply maximise your own profits. As for the carriage racing: the game imposes a strict set collection routine which equalises interaction even further. The Notre Dame, yes, that's a place where people can truly 'fight' each other. But it's a limited part of the game. Conclusion: most of the time you're on your own, so why bother at all with what the others do? My first games were with 2 players, but I doubt that things would be very different with 3 or more. (Later games with 3 players confirmed this suspicion.) The game is tantalisingly calculable: I never felt that agonising decisions were forced upon me. In some games, I played fairly calculatingly, ignoring rats for most of the game, and miraculously finished first without too much effort. There didn't seem to be anything else to do. In other games, the cards didn't cooperate, forcing all kinds of untimely decisions on me, and so I finished nearly last. Once again, there didn't seem to be anything else to do. 

In the end, Notre Dame left me on the cool side of luke warm. The busy and frilly artwork isn't particularly appealing, although the odd board shape is an eyecatcher for sure. It had me thinking of Hermagor at one point: a nearly endless source of small amounts of points which seem to accumulate no matter what you do. My sweetheart is by now very astute at reading my body language and cut me off before the first sentence left my mouth: 'We are NOT selling this game, [i]I[/i] like it!'. (I have a habit of selling games I don't like much.) Very well, the game will not be sold. But neither will it be loved very much.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
You can buy this at Boardgamebliss. It's the German edition but totally icon driven, just print the English PDF rules. US shipping isn't bad. The used asking price of this game is obscene.


Have the basegame and the expansion.

As is, very good 3 card draft game. 

Notes to self:

My problem here is the Icons on the Juggler card doesn't reflect you can move a cube from Notre Dame, the from Icon is the same as the Minstrel, just your district, where game text in the rule book explicitly leaves it out for the Minstrel but states it for the Juggler.

"The BSW rules are slightly different again: for both the minstrel and the juggler the rule says you move a cube/s from 'one of your own spaces'.
Stefan Brück has confirmed that in theory using the minstrel or juggler, you can move your own cube/s out of Notre Dame but that in practice you would never actually want to do so. Certainly with the minstrel, where you don't get to carry out the action of the new building, you would never want to move out of Notre Dame, 'which [says Stefan] is why we don't list it explicitly'."

Basically the rule is only card play moves the Trusted Friend. If you have no cubes in your personal stock you can't move your Trusted Friend.

"Situations where you can move your Trusted Friend:

1.) Playing the Trusted Friend card.
2.) Hiring the Minstrel.
3.) Hiring the Fool.

In other words, the Trusted Friend counts as an influence marker for all the persons you can hire who account for the presence or absence of influence markers.

Notably, you cannot move your Trusted Friend if you play a card but have no cubes to add to the board (thus forcing you to use a cube already on the board to do your action)."

RATING: 8
A game of prioritisation and adaptation.

I love drafting in games. It simplifies decisions, allowing us to choose between just a few choices and allows for 'fairness' and randomness to coexist.

9 action cards exist and each turn, 3 are drawn, one picked, 2 passed, 2 received, 1 picked, 1 passed, 1 recieved. You must then perform 2 of the 3 actions you hold. After 3 turns, action cards are dealt back and the whole process is repeated twice more.

This potentially denies you an action on the turn you desire - forcing you to adapt - whilst, over the course of 3 turns, giving you first pick over any card you prioritise.

The actions give rewards proportional to the number of times chosen. Whilst this encourages specialisation, the game forces generalisation by throwing a few factors at you that must be dealt with in good time. The cubes you place must first be obtained from a supply. The plague level increases each turn and will prove ruinous if too high. Money must similarly be managed.

Various actions are best used earlier or later in the game, when certain resources are running low or when underused by others. However, each seems of roughly equal benefit.

Ultimately a game of prioritisation the game has enough variables that I doubt a single strategy would ever prove unbeatable - you'll be seeing a different order of cards each game, facing different choices from opponents and will be forced to adapt.

RATING: 7
Players represent nobles, accumulating prestige in Paris.  Players deal a mini-hand of 3 cards to themselves, keep one and pass two on.  Repeat, passing one on.  Then two of the three cards they are holding can be played for actions.  Actions do the usual things - move cubes around, managing bad stuff, gain victory points and money.  Most victory points wins at the end of the nine turns.  Standard stuff, but it's quick to play and works well.  I like the modular board.

RATING: 9
Scales well from 2-5, plays quickly, very streamlined gameplay and easy to learn, enough luck to have good replay value, fantastic components, not overly brain-burning (in most cases you need to choose from one of three options, so a limited number of choices, and no analysis paralysis) - a very good light weight euro, quick play time, very favorably impressed after multiple plays.  Minus points: Not a huge amount of interaction; the cards are language independent, and don't have names of characters and buildings on the cards.
Also owned: the 9 expansion cards from the Alea [gameid=40688] - these change the long term strategies considerably, and minimize the need for cubes on the board towards the end.  

See my pictorial review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/622626/a-pictorial-overview-why-i-love-notre-dame-and-w

[q]Notre Dame is and remains an outstanding euro, and several years after graduating off the production line as part of the class of 2007, has to be considered one of the highest achievers of the light-medium games from that year, by typifying some of the best that the genre can offer. It doesn't quite have the depth of classics like Puerto Rico or Caylus, but compensates for this by being more accessible, and serves well as a somewhat lighter and quicker game that is both intuitive and elegant. Yet it's not to be underestimated or considered as a game of luck - far from it, because Notre Dame offers tense and interesting decisions that require you to manage risk and manipulate a very tight economy, and carefully construct long range plans for your point-scoring objectives. There's just the right balance between tactical choices and strategic options, and the card drafting keeps the game interactive without being overly confrontational, while the finite number of possibilities keep the game from bogging down with analysis paralysis. It's not too heavy, and yet there's also not a sense that so much strategic fat has been trimmed from the design that the end result is muddied by excessive randomness or that game-play becomes a mere shuffling of cardboard and wood with no real flavour, as is the case with some euros we've seen over the years. In many respects I suppose it is an exercise in efficiency, as many euros are, but the random draw of the cards forces you to plan different paths each game, the draft mechanic adds elements of fun and indirect interaction, and the risk management associated with the rats adds tension, all of which prevent it from being categorized with the mundane or blase. In the final analysis, this is no ordinary cube-pushing euro, and while it doesn't pretend to compete with the heavier games in the genre and won't please everyone's tastes, it remains one of the more shining examples of how good a lighter and medium weight euro really can be.

For any serious fan of Notre Dame, the new grey person cards are an absolute must have, and I highly, highly recommend them. Notre Dame has always performed strongly in our house, and the replay value and freshness offered by these expansion cards only makes it better. It's amazing what swapping in and mixing nine different cards can do! All roads do lead to Notre Dame, but as this expansion proves, there's more than one way to get there! [/q]

RATING: 7
I was really surprised by this. Its a really nicely designed game. You need to watch out and maintain your rat population or you can quickly become swamped as the population can suddenly leap up.

The action selection draft is nice and the action are simple however there are still interesting decisions to be made as you are only going to play two of the three actions.

The people each round initially seemed overpowered however the limited coins make it really interesting as it might be more advantageous to only gain 3VP and get a coin back than to plough ahead and gain 6-8VP but not get your coin back.

Notre dame itself is really nice way to churn up the victory points however it removes your cubes from the board so you then have to buy them back, to place them again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
It has it's down sides. Several. You are very limited by your card draw. If you simply don't pick up the right three cards at the right time, there's not a lot you can do. I'm not a fan of card-drafting nowadays. It's not player inter-action, it's just an exercise in doing the least worst. Some cards require a pre-condition (having money or cubes to spare) that if you can't meet, makes the card worthless to you, but the card is still too good to pass on. If you run out of money, you're stuffed. If you get into a bad cycle, you stay in a bad cycle, it's hard to work out of a poor start. If you get behind in the game, you stay behind, especially if you can't manage the rat population. You can end the game with crap, worthless choices. Not fun. All of which makes the game much heavier than it appears, or needs to be. Guess who lost. Badly. I liked the board design. Some of the actions were intersting, but there are too many options and too few opportunities. I'd say Notre Dame compares very poorly with San Marco for example, where you have the same variety of choice, but the game is stuffed with player inter-action and tough decisions, but can be played tsraight out of the box. Notre Dame is one of those games that you have to play through at least once to know how to play it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Provides a great "bang for the buck" because the price is reasonable and the game's depth is surprising given the relatively short playing time.  The design is such that you can never do everything you would like (which is a good thing).  Players need to keep an eye on rats, money, resource cubes, and VP opportunities all at the same time.

While there isn't a huge amount of interaction, there is card trading and a race for carriage tiles.  Deciding when to go into Notre Dame is key.  It provides extra points, but the point value diminishes if other players go in (a resource cube is also lost back to the general supply).  Great stuff!  Grade: B+

RATING: 7
Very smooth and easy to play, this is my new favorite of the economic Eurogames (topping St. Petersburg and Puerto Rico).  Can't wait to play more!  I like how over many games you can develop a style of play that you enjoy and explore.  It's fun to have your own board to organize.  I think the bits and large cards are fantastic!  When the itch to set up an economy and harvest victory points hits, this is the game I'll play.

Update: I haven't played it as much as I thought I would.  I need to dive back into it.  It's a game I like but I'm not sure I really 'get'.

Update:  Lowering the rating to a 7.  I like the game but it's nothing special for me.  To a degree it has the Amyitis syndrome where the board feels like a dart board of options each turn and it's hard to tell where you're going or who you are in the game.  Scattered with no central visual flow to make sense of it.  It feels abstract to me in that it's nothing more than a points engine, and points alone are not an exciting story for a board game.  At least not interesting for me to get it off the shelf very often.  Another strike against this game is the iconography, which is everywhere.  You have to decode some cards and symbols just to play.

RATING: 7
A suprisingly deep game for the short time it takes to play it. In part this is the result of the minimal player interaction. Most of the time you can already plan out your actions while everybody else is doing their stuff. While some sort of long-term strategy probably doesn't hurt the game is primarily about maximising gains with whatever hand of cards you get at the beginning of a turn. There are various means to turn those actions into prestige points, and the game needs them for the card drafting to work. Even so, the decision which card to keep and possibly play yourself is often fairly obvious. In short, a quick, light, inoffensive design that looks more complicated than it is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Notre Dame is the first Feld game I ever played. I think it's pretty neat!  Straightforward game (especially compared to his games of the last 10 years).  Lots of tough decisions to make, as there are lots of things you want to do and only a precious few actions you are allowed to take. Everything feels fluid, and I like the minimal luck factor involved but there's just enough luck with the card draws that the tension is heightened. It is mainly MPS and things never really feel tense between players. It is basically a "gain as many VPs as you can" sort of game, and the brain burn can feel a little crunchy at times. Still, it is a smart game, easy to learn, and one that could be enjoyed multiple times with the inclusion of the additional Person cards. It can feel a little punishing at times though and can leave you groggy by the end of the experience!  But that aside, it's still a signature Feld game.  Plus, the art design is really nice and the board configuration is unique!  All in all, this is a Feld classic!

RATING: 9
This is a very well designed game that I enjoyed quite a bit. Nice components, multiple paths to victory, lots of things to balance and some very nice mechanics.

Do note: It will take several plays to learn the capabilities of all the buildings and people that you can hire and I can see it getting a bit tiresome having to constantly look at the cheat sheets every move, but other than that minor flaw its a great game. If you give it the chance and play it a few times to get a feel for things and to learn the capabilities of the game. You are sure to appreciate all the thinking that must have taken place to develop this game and to have fun doing so.

This is one that takes a little time to appreciate for some, so if you don't have the time or patience to play through it a few times you may not like it. I however think it is a masterpiece of a game and am glad to have finally been able to get a copy and play it.

Many thanks to the Basement Boardgamer video review of this game here in the forums. I probably would not of bought this game until I played it or saw a video review of it first. These video reviews are a huge help when deciding on whether to buy a game or not.

RATING: 9
I don't count myself as a Stefan Feld fan, but this might be the game to convert me. This isn't a point salad, but like some of his other games, you are pulled in all different directions and have to make tough decisions about what you will do. This is made even tougher because of the card draft: you will see only three players' cards over the course of the game. You always get first choice from your cards, but you get increasingly worse leftovers from the other players, and you will take only two actions per round. Add to this a plague that you have to manage, and the game is quite simply excruciating, but in the best way. Very excited to play this some more. It's short and great.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
One of my absolute favorites. Another well designed Euro by Stephen Feld.

I was captivated with Notre Dame from the outset. It has a beautiful geometric design that changes with the number of players. The playerboards themselves function as the game board and the information they contain about players' strategies and positions is easy to glean at a glance.

Card drafting is the central mechanic. In normal Feld fashion, much work has to be done throughout the game to avert complete disaster. Every turn you feel like you need to do 4 different things to keep from moving backwards. However, you get to do two, and that is only if you choose wisely during the card drafting phase (which functions as your action selection). The player who manages to stave off the Bubonic Plague long enough to earn victory points will win. You must try hard to play ND well. I enjoy that challenge each game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

The down side is that there's not a lot of player interaction; you do have to be aware of what the person on your left is doing, and it's helpful to keep track of what cards the people to your right have gone through, but you're not really engaging with other people's personalities.

RATING: 6.33
After all the great buzz this game got I had to try it myself (prelimenary rating after one 5 player game). To make it short: it left me lukewarm. A lot of people praised that the theme is not superficial but I found a lot of actions being mechanics without an actual connection to the building you use or the person you bribe. Furthermore the cardboard is too thin so that it begins to warp after 5mins putting out of the box.
Rating after two plays (4/5 Player); I am willing to play again and rating may change, but not much. I am still waiting for Alea to be back with a great gamers game.

RATING: 10
Reminds me a lot of Puerto Rico.  There seems to be a lot of potential paths to victory but you must adapt on the fly based on what cards you draw (and when you draw them) and what cards are passed to you.  There isn't as much randomness in the game as it might seem on your first play; the game also provides many ways to mitigate bad luck. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fairly easy to explain, and works well with 2-5 players.  Balance is important -- don't want to run out of money else you can't hire at the end of the turn or contribute to Notre Dame; probably not good to run out of markers in your local supply; probably want to pick up some messages to stop other players from getting them; definitely want some markers in the park to increase your score on each scoring play.  Interesting card passing mechanic -- you can choose a card to use yourself, or just to keep it from your left-hand neighbour.  Common dilemma:  should you keep a card that is useless to you just to stop your neighbour from getting it?

Plays quickly -- easily 60 minutes including explanations, but maybe as little as 30 mins for experienced players in a 3-player game.

One important thing to keep track of is rats:

In the 3 A turns: 12 rats (average of 4 rats/turn)
In the 3 B turns: 13 rats (average of 4.33 rats/turn)
In the 3 C turns: 12 rats (average of 4 rats/turn)

The total is 37 rats, less the 9 spaces that you have on your rat track (in the harbour).  That means that during the game, you have to kill or prevent 28 rats to not get plague penalties.  Now, it's not terrible to get penalized (lose 2 points and lose an influence marker from the most populated borough on your board), but losing too many pieces that way can cost you the game.

If you see a 7- or 8-rat turn, you might consider maxing out and taking the penalty -- or taking the doctor (if available), which zeroes out all the new rats but still lets you use the hospital to decrease on the rat track.  If the doctor is worth 7 rats, that's only 21 more you have to kill in the rest of the game, which could be done with 3 gradually-placed markers in the hospital.  (Of course, the heavy-rat turns are what kill you in the plague aspect of this game.)

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game explains the plague in Europe.

You gain fame/VP by controlling Notre Dame.

You gain gold/influence cubes to place on locations to get gold/more influence cube/reduce plague values on your borough.

A game on area of influence / maintaining a clean city.

Easy for new gamers

RATING: 8.5

After a few plays, the game still feels solid, if a little dependent on the whims of the player to your right. The fact that there's a drafting element makes sure that this 'right player dominance' is not quite as pronounced as in Medina, Samurai, or Puerto Rico, making the game easier to play with different skill levels.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a good game, but man... I hate it, because I have yet to do well at it. I always feel miserable afterwards, such that I want to smash some dishes or something. I'm thus not inclined to expand any further other than to say it is a good game, with agonizing choices, and multiple paths to victory. Initial rating: "8".

Update (11/13/10) 6 plays: After playing a number of games from the dark ages, dealing with themes that deal with plague, rats, pollution, starvation, and graves, I like this one the best, when compared to World Without End, Antiquity, and Agricola. Still can't win, though.

Update (02/11/10): With Hansa Teutonica out there, why should I ever play this again? Dropping to a "6".

Update (01/31/2014) 8 plays: Took over three years to bring this back to the table. I am reminded why I do not like this game. It is very unforgiving, chaotic, and punitive. I made a "small" mistake of running out of money in the first period, and after that suffered for the rest of the game. Stephen Feld has been derided for his "point salad" designs, and this is another example. It seems his design philosophy is introduce a massive amount of chaos at the players, either through dice (Castles of Burgundy) or pass-the-trash card drafting of Notre Dame, and then giving the players a huge menu of choices for VPs. The mechanisms seem like they are pasted on after the mechanisms have been developed, so I spend half the game wondering why what the significance of "this" action versus "that". In the end, it is all about maximizing VPs, and the theme really is irrelevant.

Like Puerto Rico, money is very important early on in the game, and then players need to convert their engine over to generating VPs later in the game. Trying the keep the rats infestation down is important, but it shouldn't be the sole focus. There are a number of things players want to do, and not enough actions to do them, in typical Euro fashion. I was very frustrated at the end of this game. The others suspected that it would be several more years before this hit the table again. They may be right.

Update (02/02/2014) 9 plays: After cooling down for a day, I requested this game back to the table. Focusing on leveraging the cards given to me, I was able to pull off a significant win. I did not worry too much about the rats, as I had in past games, allowing the rats to get to max, and then keeping it there. In one instance, we were all blown out of the water with seven rats in one turn, but losing 2 VP and a cube didn't seem like a big deal. I am beginning to appreciate the game design, now that I am beginning to understand Feld's thinking. His games are swirling chaos of randomness, and one just needs to accept that. Within that swirl, the player that can maximize leverage on VP generation will do well. It also helps to know what cards are available, and what cards are coming up. I will up this up to a tentative "7". I'm still cautious of Feld's designs, but may end up liking some of his games.

Update (02/14/2014) 10 plays: Won again, by completely ignoring the hospital and the plague. Concentrated on generating cubes and moving the caravan around to gather up all of the chits, which would eventually turn into 6-9 VP later for one of the bonus cards. Was hit with the plague 5-6 times, but still won with a margin of 5-6 VP, which tells me that the hospital is overrated. However, these games were where the doctor bonus card came out on the first round, rendering the hospital strategy useless--there is a bonus card which benefits the player who manages to keep the rats under control. Knowing what the future bonus cards are, and playing specifically towards one (or two) of them could be the margin for victory. On an unrelated note, the rotating player boroughs, oriented specifically for a given number of players, is pure design genius.

RATING: 7.4
聖母院倫抽卡片與七大不同，是先輪抽再進行遊戲
遊戲進行一回合簡單分以下:
開人物卡>輪抽卡片>依序執行兩張卡片>購買人物卡執行>鼠疫爆發
(人物卡是分一張該時期腳色+兩張中立卡)
1.鼠疫爆發時，需失去兩分，且鼠疫不會歸零，會停留在9
2.每時期結算，需鼠疫爆發玩，才會進行結算。

擴充人物卡
A時期：
經理Manager(2)：目前所得總分加倍。
學者Scholar(1)：立刻執行第三張動作卡。
護士Nurse(4)：由場外拿1個方塊到醫院，並將鼠疫指示物下降1格。

B時期：
間諜Spy(1)：看灰色人物卡的順序後原順序放回，並得2分。
吉普賽Gypsies(2)：選擇自己任意數量的方塊(不能是親信)退回場外。
車伕Coachman(3)：從蒐集的信件板塊中，選擇一種板塊立刻全部執行。

C時期：
旅館老闆Host(3)：旅館裡每有1個方塊得2分。
守衛Guard(1)：自己的場外每有2個方塊得3分。
顧問Advisor(2)：分別額外付1元給其他2個人物，並執行他們的能力。(可付給1人2次，或2人各1次，但不可付給顧問本身)

RATING: 7.2
Simple card drafting game.

The board is unique in that it is like individual player boards that "interlock" seamlessly based on the player count (3-5).

There is one space that seems kind of weird--the space where you only get one thing for playing there, not # of things equal to # of cubes there (like all the other spaces). I understand why the space is there--it's a general get-one-of-whatever space, but having the effect of "if you have 4[?] cubes there, you can get the benefit of that space twice" is strange because it actually makes the space sound like it should be valuable or attractive when it still is a bad deal. Sometimes confuses new players. I try to stress that it is [generally] the worst space on the board.

Oh and rats! I mean, keep the rats away. Rats!

RATING: 7
Mechanically this is about as simple as a Eurogame can get: place a cube and get the corresponding benefit. It's interesting in that you're perpetually short of both cubes and cash, and have to generate more while optimising your play. It's pretty much themeless and the card-passing mechanism can hand the game to an opponent if you're careless about what you pass on. I'm sure I'll play it regularly, but it's dry, dry, dry.

RATING: 9
The first thing to understand is that this is really a card game in disguise.  Then: Presents many options, plays quickly, and finds really interesting (but subtle) ways to provide player interaction. There are many clever things about this game--the limited nature of cubes, the way Notre Dame works, the importance of the card passing, the additive nature of the districts... I like it a lot.

Update: When I finally win a game, I am often tempted to revise its rating downward.  In the case of Notre Dame, I raised it, because there are so many interesting things going on in this game and even though I won decisively, there were a couple of decisions that I had seriously considered making differently.

Further update: DO NOT underestimate the importance of the cards you pass your opponents! Notre Dame puts their fate in your hands in a way that few board games (as opposed to card games) do.

RATING: 8
Played with 2, 3 and 5 players. Notre Dame pushes all the right buttons in terms of game mechanics; it delivers a mid-weight dose of resource management within a manageable time frame. It work wells with 2 players which is a bonus for a multiplayer game. However, it is a disappointment in terms of theme. At no point in the game do I feel that I am managing a city quarter, let alone one in Paris. Apart from the box cover art and the Notre Dame tiles there is very little to associate this game with Paris; indeed the board artwork could be of a German city. The board tesselation is cool but pointless (its only serves to allow coach movement between city quarters - a mechanic that itself feels tacked on). And the wooden components are dull: the hatted figure from so many other games (Princes of Florence, San Marco...), a coach that looks like a police car and lots of small cubes. UDPATE: after a few plays my opinion of the game has become more positive. It also works well with 5 players, especially as the playing time is no more than 1.5 hours.

RATING: 7.5
4 plays - 3 FTF, 3,4 & 5 players

A pretty good one hour middle-weight on par with Louis and Mykerinos, slightly similiar in play as it is a placement game with very limited actions but people are not competing in the same areas as you which makes a big difference interaction-wise, there is some but it is limited to the carriage items up for grabs, Notre Dame and the card drafting. It is interesting and excellent that you are playing against the other players AND the game itself re the Rats and the card draw.

A good dose of luck in the card draw but just about right for the game length and weight(1 hour), sometimes you pick up 3 good cards of your 9 all at once and you are forced to pass 2 which does suck, if your opponents don't share this same fate then they pass you scraps but it seems to go around fairly evenly. The card drafting is similar to Midguard, start with some cards, grab one and pass them to the left etc and had the same negative aspect where you see the same junk being passed around, it seems too samey as well as the same cards just keep getting re-hashed (also like Midguard) but it works for the most part.  

We did think the Inn was very weak in our games and no one used it akin to the University in PR and could probably use tweaking. On the other hand the Park seems pretty strong giving 1 extra bonus VP every time you get points to the point if someone has it, you better too. Other than this everything looks very balanced and you need to play a pretty balanced game to win managing the Rats, scooping things up with your carriage, keeping money coming in, keeping cubes coming in, and being able to capitalize on bonus points where possible all while having somewhat of a strategy as well which makes this enjoyable to play. It can also be a very tight and unforgiving game but that's part of it's charm.

Not too heavy on strategy or deep but a good multiple path middleweight with a fast pace that comes in at around an hour so a good game to keep around, I do suspect my rating could fall a little over time. It will take at least one game for most to get a grasp of strategy and 3 players might be best (fastest and most control)although 4 and 5 was also quite good and I really want to try 2. I do find I get the same kind of feeling/satisfaction as Caylus (Dry theme, worker placement, buying favours) only this has the luck factor albeit it is also a much shorter game 

[u]UPDATE[/u]: 8 => 7.5  Still a very nice and fairly meaty one hour game, I love the tightness of the cubes, money and the rats but I hate the luck of the draw that can happen especially if you get all three of your good cards in one draw or not getting a money card when you really need it.  Now that I have played in the Year of the Dragon lowering my rating was an easier decision (only a little) and I question whether or not I will keep this after owning ITYOTD, they do scratch the same itch. 

[u]UPDATE[/u]: Still keeping this after many many plays of both, quite similiar but both good in their own way

2012-06-20

Initial Rating : 5.0 (July 2011)

I just found out that my first Notre Dame game had a BIG rules error, when the rats hit the 9 space, we moved them to the 1 space making the game boring and without tension, however with the correct set of rules this is a very tight game with very hard decisions, a winner in my book. It went from a 5.0 rating to a 8.0 and become my second favorite Feld behind In the Year of the Dragon.

The theme is a little boring but the tactical gameplay is excellent, it has a relatively short playtime but it is a delight to play. Stefan Feld always deliver innovative games (although I don’t love all his games).

For now I prefer to play it with 4 players and I want to play it again soon.

2015-05-01

After more plays of Notre Dame and in the Year of the Dragon, I can say that now I prefer In the Year of the Dragon, however in the last couple of years Feld has designed lots of fun games (I have yet to play Aquasphere and La Isla) and I enjoy most of them, so I must name myself a Feld fan.

I continue to like Notre Dame, it has easy rules but the decisions are far from obvious. There is some luck with the order of the cards (the ones you draw and the personalities) but it is a more forgiving game than In the Year of the Dragon. Due to this, it is perfect to introduce to new gamers. Love the draft and majorities mechanics and it plays very fast. Without the rats the game would be garbage, but the rats increase the tension during the game making it a solid design.

Bottom line, a good drafting game I’m usually willing to play.

RATING: 8
My favorite game in the 45-75 min time slot.  A little solitairish, but for a game of that length I'm okay with that.  Very tactical.

In the pursuit of points there are three major resources to manage:  gold, influence cubes, and rats.  Rats do not seem to have an exact 1:1 equivalence with gold and cubes but shift in value as the game situation changes.  That small mechanical tweak on an exact equivalence rescues the resource management portion of the game from staleness.

The variable powers of the personages, the give-and-take of the cathedral card, and the drafting are other appealing aspects of the game which raise it from "just another Euro" into something resembling greatness, or at least extreme goodness.

I tend to play Notre Dame in bursts, about 6-7 games in a month or two before shelving it for a few months.  It might not stay fresh for me without the breaks.

RATING: 7
Believe it or not, my first play of this game happened on Nov 3rd, 2008! 

It's been in the groups forever, but I hadn't been able to get in on a game before hand. I was always in a different game when another table fired it up.

So, after finally being able to exhale and savor the play I can say this:
 
 -- It played very smooth and I enjoyed it.

I liked the trading mechanism...really, card drafting mechanism (here are 3 cards, choose one and pass the others on, now here are 2 other cards from the player on your right; choose 1 and pass the other on).

I'm not sure how the game will hold up long term, but I can't judge it without playing it some more--and I'm willing to play it again to find out. 

My initial misgivings on the long term play is that it will feel...processional or like you can just kind of cost along in play.

I put in a good showing, but not great. I was running a very strong VP ([b]V[/b]ictory [b]P[/b]oint) engine by the end of the game; it was chugging away and I was making up ground at a great clip--time just ran out.

The main Achilles's heel for me in this session was missing out on a couple of bribing rounds, but such were the cards that kept me from getting gold a couple of times. 

One other thought on my first play learning: I did have the rats under great control by the end game rounds. This allowed me a [b]very[/b] free range of play, but on reflection, it also hampered me getting it in place. I should have rode the edge of the rats a bit more.

On the whole, it seems to be a pretty good game. I did enjoy it.

RATING: 3
Painstakingly detailed, lovingly reverential homage to medieval ecclesiastic architecture. Aw, just kidding folks, it's another generic cube shuffler, with a drawing of a church on the box.

In this one you're shuffling cubes to score points, get money, and get more cubes. You use the money to trigger card effects to score points, get money, and get more cubes. Most efficient shuffling wins.

While the card passing introduces some element of player interaction, there's nothing here I haven't seen a dozen times before. 

Maybe I'm just getting very jaded about this sort of thing. Maybe I need to take up a new hobby.

RATING: 7
I like luck elements in games.  I enjoy a game where a player never feels that he cannot win... right down to the final turns.

Notre Dame is such a game.  Random draws from your action stack will determine your strategies for the entire game.  This luck factor is mitigated somewhat by the passing of the cards, but your initial 3 card draw will determine your destiny.

Considering the alea 'big box' line, I'd place Notre Dame squarely in the middle.  After last year's 'Rum & Pirates,' which I enjoyed immensely, this was a bit of a let down.  However, it will definitely be played more at the Club.

It still rates an easy '7' from me.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Getting introduced to Notre Dame now feels a bit like stepping through a time machine to a different era in board games. No doubt, it is a well-designed game. Each round starts with a short bit of card drafting and then two cards are played for actions with a third action available for purchase from three personage cards that are changed every round. Most actions involve placing influence cubes in your borough to get points, more cubes, money, or to control the negative effects of the plague. At the end of each round, players increase their plague track by the number of rats on all of the displayed personage cards. If you have to extend off the end of the track, you lose points and an already placed cube. Most points after 9 rounds wins.

The game is about controlling the flow of cubes and money in order to generate points and plague protection. While a pass 'n draft card mechanic probably felt fresh 4 years prior to the publication of 7 Wonders, it feels anything but today. (Fairy Tale, I think, was the first game to introduce this mechanic and was published two years prior to Notre Dame.) Keeping a consistent flow of cubes and money and controlling the constant pressure of the plague is challenging to be sure, but I feel like this game has been surpassed in ingenuity in the last 10 years and it lacks the interactivity that generally keeps me interested in some of the older designs. Good, but it doesn't feel special enough to set it apart today.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This was one of my first games since joining BGG. 

One thing that distinguishes ND is the depth/pace ratio. It plays fast. It also gives you several  long-term options for developing your board (score points), but the fact that you're passing cards and responding to fluctuations in the plague provides some good short term decisions. Not a forgiving game for newer gamers.

Essentially, the game gives you six areas to develop that provide incrementally better advantages each time you build them up. For example, the first time you develop your banks, you will receive a payout of one gold. Second time: two gold. Etc. The game also gives you limited room to shift investments. For example, three times in the game, you'll have the opportunity to downgrade one area and upgrade another, receiving the new benefit in the process. Three other times in the game, you'll have the opportunity to shift a big chunk of your investment without receiving the benefit. Say, for example, you're rushed the bank development early and are flush with cash for the remainder of the game. You can pull the plug and reinvest in the residential district, setting you up for major gains later. The particular mode of specialization, both in overall strategy and in mid-game shifts, separates Notre Dame from the pack. But I do think it becomes well worn after a while (~20 plays). 

Update: With experience, this really needs to be a brisk, 45-60 minute game. And even at that weight, I'm convinced that Vikings blows it out of the water. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
It's true.  The more you play it, the more you see what the game has to offer.  In some ways it is a straightforward economic system - the more you invest in an area, the more it becomes worth to you.  However, choices regarding the short supply of cubes which help to enhance the value of an action, along with the card-passing mechanism in which your fate is somewhat controlled by others, makes this a game which requires a challenging mix of planning and adaptation.
