RATING: 9.5
It's hard to believe there was a time I wanted to trade this away.  This is clearly more and more becoming one of my favorite games.  Quick play time, very satisfying tactical and strategic decisions.  I think one of my favorite aspects is the balace between strategy and tactics.  It's somewhat hard for me to describe this balance.  It's not a game where you can have a major over arching strategy like you can in Caylus, at least not to the same magnitude.  However, how you use your cards each round are not purely tactical either, at least not in the immediate sense.  There's almost a hint of deduction in this game, as more cards are revealed you can anticipate what will come up soon, thus it becomes a game of short goals and maximizing your opportunity turn by turn.

RATING: 9
Excellent abstract game by the designer who brought us the overwhelmingly random Rum and Pirates. The rules were not as heavy as I first thought, and the game can be played fairly quickly once players know how.

There's a nice balance between the interaction with other players (cars and Notre Dame) and keeping a players own region free of rats. While the basic symbols were easy to understand, I felt that the other cards needed text on them (Alea helpfully provided a cheat sheet for players to refer to, but some text -- even a name on the card -- would've been brilliant).

One of the better games to have been released from Alea in a while. I'd happily play this again.

RATING: 8
I liked the idea to make an action more useful the more you use it. It is very important to have cubes to perform actions - they are obtained with actions. However, it is not enough cubes for all actions, so several times they have to be replaced from other actions. The less such replacements, the better. Another important resource is money. Lack of money lessens the choices, e.g., you cannot employ helpers, which are generally pretty helpful. And finally, it is very important to control the number of rats so that you won't be forced to give away victory points.

Man patiko idėja, kad kuo daugiau kartų veiksmą kartoji, tuo jis tampa naudingesnis. Žaidime labai svarbu nuolat turėti kubelių veiksmams atlikti - jie atsiranda vykdant veiksmus. Bet visiems veiksmams jų neužtenka, taigi kelis kartus juos tenka perstatyti iš vieno veiksmo į kitą. Reikia siekti, kad perstatymų būtų kuo mažiau. Taip pat svarbūs ir pinigai. Jų trūkumas taip pat užveria tam tikras galimybes, pavyzdžiui, samdyti pagalbininkus, kurie dažnai būna gana naudingi. Na ir svarbu nepamiršti kontroliuoti žiurkių, kad nereikėtų atiduoti pergalės taškų.

RATING: 8
I see this one as a quite an innovative mix of mechanisms. The game board is somewhat unusual, the draft element is a smooth randomizer, you'll always have to pay attention to the rats so that your other plans won't backfire and the resource management of money and cubes functions nicely as resources tend to be a bit scarce. Drafting element however might shut someone out of money and cubes, although you should prepare yourself for such a possibility in advance. I've played this one with experienced (of this) players and I really can't see the point of the carts in this game. The other problem is that the game quite fast devolves into a meaningless attempting at gathering just victory points instead of building your board position as the game doesn't favor the latter option.

RATING: 8
There are many ways to get victory points and win, and you need to balance other resources besides victory points.

In a way, it's like Puerto Rico.  You need money in order to do things, and you have to take actions in order to get money, but the money is not the end goal.

  There's actually more going on here than in PR.  In adition to getting money, one also has to get the small person-cubes in order to do anything, and also try to keep rats at bay.  

But this is also simpler to grasp, easier to play, and much more fun than Puerto Rico.

I've only played this once.

RATING: 8
Based on 50+ plays.  ND packs a good deal of strategy into a short time-frame.  The decisions are usually interesting, and there are a number of viable paths to victory.  I just wish the card-drafting offered a little more scope for tactics and strategy, a la Magic drafts.  With only 3 cards, I often feel like the decision is not as agonizing as I might like it to be.  The player interaction is a also bit thin.  Sure, you can counter-draft to hurt the person to your left, but this tactic often shoots you in the foot.  Still, ND is all in all a solid game.

I actually enjoy this game on BSW, unlike most of their 'ports.  I think it's the ridiculously short play time - we're talking a full two-player game in under 10 minutes.  It's the perfect study break.

RATING: 9
The Good: A real challenge to balance everything and therefore there were tough decisions every turn. Multiple paths to victory. Seemed shorter than most heavy stragey games. The board and cards all have icons which are very useful. Card artwork was pretty good.

The Bad: Easy for new players to accidentally make game-breaking decisions such as not having enough gold for hiring. The overall board leaves a very bland impression, which is one reason I was not eager to play. That is a shame, because if you look closely, there are some really nice icons, and there is a lot going on. Also, it has the weirdest shaped board that I have ever seen. That would be okay, except that it comes in 4 pieces which you have to then puzzle over how to get it to fit together.

RATING: 7.5
Very fun solitaire, but I want to fling rats at my opponents.

The only influence you have over others is driving the SUV (cart) to steal tiles before others and card drafting that you pass down the table...but most likely you're just dumping the cards you don't want yourself vs. trying to fudge your opponent and the tiles you're just trying to get for yourself first which puts in urgency, not conflict.

Too Euro friendly for me to give it a 9, but overall a great max/min puzzle that scales well.

RATING: 8
Building up and trying to avoid the plague.  That is the way things work in Notre Dame.  Choose cards that you draw and from others as you pass choices around and try to gain the most victory points by the end of the game.

RATING: 8
Very good low-on-luck resource management game. As such, there are no breathtaking new elements, but the familiar mechanisms have been blended together into a neat design. Build up an economy to produce blocks and money while managing the rats plague and then start collecting victory points. Don't get fixed on any particular one of the different roads to victory, but try to adapt according to the cards you get and the gameplay of the other players (avoid pursuing the same strategy as your righthand neighbour).

RATING: 7.5
[2008.Feb.17] Notre Dame by Stefan Feld is the 11th game in the Alea big box game series. This action selection [you choose between 2 of 3 available actions] and resource management [you manage your influence (cubes) and coins] game feels rather lightweight, but is by no means easy. On top of the pressure of always feeling like you never have enough influence and coins, each of the game's nine turns ends with the effects of the dreaded rats. I hope you have invested in your borough's hospital. Beware the plague!

[2016.Feb.2] This doesn't include as much pain in a game box as does [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/31594]In the Year of the Dragon[/url] (another Stefan Feld title), but it still makes players squirm. Darn those plague rats! {grin}

But I think the problem with notre dame is that you have so much different ways to score that you do not necessarily do it the optimal way. You just fix yourself an objective and try to follow it. So there is simply no excitement to play that game.

RATING: 8
There's a blending of multiple strategic paths towards victory.  For newbies, I would strongly advise to pay good attention to the 3 bribery roles that come up every round and not to miss any bribery of roles if possible.  For experienced players, it will usually be close games with the card deck randomness and card counting memory of each player determining the outcome of most games.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Solid card drafting game in which you seek to put together the best points generating machine possible.  Seven different areas in your region each do different actions to a quantity dependent on how much influence you have present.  One can also score points by placing influence in Notre Dame itself although a tithe is required.  Money tends to be a bit tight and one must always be aware of the rat problem which can come back to bite you (although its possible to develop a points engine in which you will just not care too much about the rats).  In the card drafting, you take the top 3 cards from your personal stack, pick one, and pass the other two to the next player.  You pick one from the two cards a person passes you and then take the remaining card left over from the third person.  This primarily tactical in that you must always make the best of the situation that presents itself with people giving you cards that may or may not fit into your master plan.  There are times that I feel this game becomes fairly mechanical and almost robotic as usually it is fairly obvious which cards you should or should not play.

This all results in a very tense and sometimes brainburning game which I liked but after 2 plays was enough for my wife. As of today it is no longer in my collection.

RATING: 8
Enjoyable, with the characters coming in a different order each time, but a bit too light, compared to another relatively short euro like Keyflower.

When played with 2 players, more enjoyable than Caylus Magna Carta, for my wife and I.  There are more things to keep an eye on: the plague, the special characters, the influence, the gold, the places where influence is exerted,...

Also has a slight taste of Caylus Magna Carta: keeping only one card and passing the other two to your neighbor is a variation on worker placement (you can prevent some of your opponents from performing a given action); you need resources in order to develop things (coins, the hospital,...); the possible actions change as the game develops (the special power given by the cards, in Notre Dame).

Nice time length (about the same as Race for the Galaxy, for 2).

RATING: 8
Well - another Stefan Feld game (paradoxally I got to know his games only through this year's Im Jahre des Drachen) that I like. At first glance the interaction between the players seems small but then you realize that you have to pass action cards to other players - directly influencing their choices and that's virtually as much interaction in a game as you can wish for. There's a Goa-like feeling to improving your districts and the influence cubes with the division into two supplies remind me of El Grand but that's OK. All in all I am positively surprised by this title especially since I bought it only on the basis of some opinions I've heard.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Unfortunately, the luck factor is just a tad too much for a game of this weight (light-heavyweightish) and complexity,  especially since it undercuts what potentially is some strategically interesting decisions. This is exacerbated by the seeming weak-sister nature of the Inn. Nevertheless, there are several interesting paths to victory, numerous interlocking systems, which in combination are very fresh, and the rat/plague system is, if not unique, certainly different enough to grab your attention, and it also creates a nice tension by providing an interesting variation on the "currency" concept.

The 'wow' factor of the physical design and the excellent scalability pushes Notre Dame over the top to being a buy when it comes out in English.

[It's actually rather tempting to bump it up another point just for the sheer entertainment value of seeing how enthusiastic John Brier has become about ND. Playing the game last night was the online equivalent of seeing a kid bounce up and down in his seat while waiting for a favourite snack :) ]

UPDATE: Yeah, the hyperbole monster bit me. ND is a good design, and I expect to be playing it on Brettspielwelt for a long time to come, but it just doesn't quite match up to the other games in this category. Ra, Saint Petersburg (post-expansion), Blue Moon City, Torres...they are all significantly more elegant and mostly less prone to twists of fate. Drawing your Bank and Seminary together in Section A of the game is a large blow, and drawing one of them with the Hospital is only marginally less annoying. The shattering weakness of the Hotel and the need to commit fully to the Residence in order to make it worthwhile are lesser, but nagging, flaws as well.

Fun, but not a buy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A very good Stefan Feld game, but one where you are always struggling to keep disaster at bay.  The basic mechanic of the game is card drafting to take a particular action.  You own a district in Paris and you are trying to keep it from the plague while at the same time advancing its prosperity.  If you are not keen on games that on games where you are struggling with very significant "slaps" inflicted on players by the game, this may not be for you. 

I really like the game, but I am Stefan Feld fan.  This is one of his games that is a little divisive where gamers who don't like to live on the cusp of disaster really dislike it. 

It is a medium-weight Euro.  It is not family friendly but very close to be.  If you had teenagers who like Euros, I see this one going over well.

RATING: N/A
See Farid Widjaya's Review: http://vimeo.com/936821

Recommended as a stepping stone game to Caylus.
Perhaps the following in order:

- STONE AGE
- NOTRE DAME (See http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/193778)
- PUERTO RICO
- Pillars of the Earth
- Agricola
- CAYLUS

NB: PILLARS OF THE EARTH (sometimes referred to as Caylus Lite) and AGRICOLA (sometimes referred to as PR Heavy) were also mentioned in other reviews of worker placement and resource management games. The games mentioned above, however, were chosen b/c there seems to be universal appeal and a good balance between light to heavy gaming (as well as shorter to longer playing) as you move up the ladder (i.e., f/SA to Caylus).

NB: Be aware that in using the above games as a stepping stone, that SA and PotE collect resources BUT do not manage them (as does ND, PR and Caylus)

NB: Caylus (unlike the other games) scales down unusually well to a very satisfying 2 player game. Whereas the others seems to be more enjoyable at the 3-4 player mark.

Despite it's high rating, I'm pushing this to a "2 - Love to Have" (vs. 1) since it's game board might not appeal to kids as much. Since we already have PR and Caylus (and SA seems to be a big hit already - in large part due to its fun element and nice looking/feeling theme), it seems that it's worth jumping to PR rather than waiting around until we have a copy of ND. Also, Pillars might be worth getting (and playing) at this juncture (or right after PR) since it also has a beautiful gameboard and theme. And, in a similar vein, it might be worth jumping Agricola (which seems to be a VERY HEAVY version of Stone Age). Therefore, the new path to Caylus might look like this:

- STONE AGE
- PUERTO RICO
- PILLARS OF THE EARTH
- CAYLUS

Save Notre Dame and Agricola for later.
NB: If PR doesn't hit on the first go round, ND may be worth trying (since it has been regarded as a nice "Puerto Rico lite")

RATING: 9
This is an easy-to-play game that combines a number of mechanics, including area control, card drafting, and hand management. The strategy is subtle, but intriguing. The game plays well with seemingly any number of players (2-5), although it seems that specialization is less of an option with two players (not a criticism). Any fan of Euros should enjoy this quite a lot, but it likely won't appeal to others.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Very elegant card drafting + worker placement mechanic, with the possibility of some strong area manipulation using the person cards. This game is a gem in its simplicity and depending how you play it can be either very light or quite thoughtful. 
For sure it has a strong tactical connotation but it can also be strategic since you are aware of which cards will be displayed during each phase and therefore you can plan  powerful combos.
The unpredictable rising of the plague adds some nice tension and will force you to "waste" some point-generating actions to counter its effect; so you can choose whether playing more aggressively, trying to generate points regardless of the plague effect, or more conservatively.

RATING: 8
The limited actions (18 played cards in total) really limits you (in a good way). Apart from that, you're constantly down on resources (cubes and money). In the midst of that all you're trying to gather as much prestige points as you can.

The concept of taking and passing cards gives options to make the best of your actions and also influence the options for your left neighbor. With less experienced players this can result in a seating order problem, but we didn't have any problems with it.

RATING: 6.5
I enjoy playing it, but I feel like this game may be a bit less balanced than it gives the impression of being.  Though there are several options for actions each turn, at any given moment only one or two of them really make sense for any particular player.  Some of the actions are just fallback options, the lame thing you do when you didn't get the card for the cool thing you really should be doing.  I do like the fact that points are hidden--scoring tracks are just demoralizing.  I like to be able to think I'm winning right up until I don't.  My favorite feature in this game is the rats drawn on the cards to represent the plague.  Really nice art, high production values.

RATING: 5
Randomness of cards can be fun, when you can do some significant action with ANY card. In that case, important is not what I get, but not what I do with it. Then there can be some control. It's not like that in this game. Good action is obvious depending on situation - and what you can do is hoping you get that card or your right person gives it to you. Long-term strategy is just impossible.

Limitation on action does not always make a game tense, sacarcity of resource does not always make a game engaging, abundance of choice does not always make a game interesting. All those character contribute to the dry and frustrating nature of this game.

Thematically pointless, too.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Even just one play (and one in which I was beaten badly) is enough to see that this is a gem of a game: multiple paths to victory, a nice balance of strategy and tactics, a devilish need to balance many concerns, high turn angst (I always wanted to execute all three cards in my hand, not just two), a surprisingly compact play time, great iconography (we rarely needed the people reference sheet after the first half of the game), and an absolutely gorgeous board and components make Notre Dame stand out for me.

Despite not having much in common with In the Year of the Dragon, I can still feel a connection -- and that's a great thing. I love the interplay of the plague mechanic with the other elements, and the important of each element of the game manifested itself clearly as we played.

With a few more plays, I can see my rating going up -- there's a lot under the hood, and a lot of diverse mechanics that don't seem like they'd go together, but do, to take into account. Really, really nifty.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The 'Alea style' in this one is so strong that it can't possibly be mistaken for a game by any other publisher. Sure, it's interesting, but in the end I find it boiling down to the classic "play cards to get cubes on the board to fuel the victory point machine" kind of game. Basically, the game is a somewhat more involved Louis XIV with card drafting thrown in and any other player interaction taken out. It's a 'solitaire' area influence game in the sense that you're constantly trying to optimize the distribution of the cubes in your quarter of the town as required by the specific phase of the game. But the game played very well (as far as I can say after only one play) and was pretty tense and exciting at times.

RATING: 10
Wow, my new favorite Feld. Remarkably fast playing, with easy to grasp mechanics. The card drafting not only gives a little player interaction, it also prohibits Analysis Paralysis by allowing players only three actions to choose from. I enjoy the way the boroughs are divided into slices, which also act as roadways for the carriage. And while that's a small thing, i can't help but to be reminiscent of Letters from Whitechapel. I also really enjoy the threat of the rats and how you have to deal with them. Definitely my new go-to Feld game to teach to others, as it has a low barrier of entry, and plays fast.

RATING: 7

Paris, fin du XIV siècle. Dans l'ombre de la majestueuse cathédrale Notre-Dame, vous jouez le rôle d'un influent citoyen, rivalisant avec les autres joueurs pour faire rejaillir sur vous le prestige du saint lieu. En prenant la tête d'un quartier de la capitale, vous devrez batailler ferme pour acquérir le contrôle des banques, auberges et autres bâtiments qui vous donneront divers avantages sur vos adversaires.

 Vous obtiendrez prospérité et renommée en allant chercher le soutien de différents personnages que vous devrez corrompre en utilisant votre or. Ce faisant vous apporterez votre contribution à Notre-Dame, dont le rayonnement sera le symbole de votre consécration. Mais surtout prenez garde à bien protéger vos concitoyens contre la menace incessante de la peste, car à trop vouloir gagner, il y'a souvent beaucoup à perdre !
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Initial rating after first 2 plays: 6,5 Nice game in Puerto Rico / El Grande / Louis XIV Eurostyle. Works with 2 as well. Once you get the hang of it, it plays a lot faster than the usual gamer's euro. Theme is pasted on, but not as bad as the average Knizia. Doesn't matter to me that much (I can appreciate a good abstract as well) I like the nice looking modular board & artwork. The hardest decision is which card too keep and which too give away to your neighbour. That's the only real player interaction part as well, but it's a constant confrontation....

Revisited this game a couple of years later, and obviously my taste in games has changed, or this has aged really fast. Felt like an average slightly boring euro this time. Downgraded my rating. Glad I never bought it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A solid Feld design, built around a simple but effective card draft. You try to build up efficiency in different areas, as each time you take an action in an area you add a cube to said area and for every cube in the area when you take the action, the better it will be. But you depend on getting access to these actions, which all depend on what you receive in the card draft. Each player draws three cards from their own personal deck, chooses one and hands the rest to the next player, out of the three cards they wind up with they will only play two.

At the end of each round you can potentially pay to gain a some kind of bonus, and you manage the rat count in your borough. At the end of the game whoever has the most points wins the game.

Only reason I've sold my copy is because I decided to buy the 10th anniversary edition (for the additional content)

I'm not sure whether the randomness of the card drawing is a good or a bad thing; it makes it just that much harder to try to carry out a plan. More playing is needed.

RATING: 9
Nice edition to my Alea collection.  I really don't mind the multi-player solitaire aspect although the card passing does give you a chance to really help another player if you are not careful. A two-player game on BSW takes only 10-15 minutes while yielding lots of strategic challenge. My strategy tip for new players is to focus on filling the hospital with 3 cubes as quickly as possible, get as many cubes as possible into play, and always have a gold available for bribing each round.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of Feld's first big Euro hits and for many it is up there with his best.

In Notre Dame the players are rich families that own whole boroughs that surround the grand cathedral in Paris. 

Each turn they are trying to improve their own Burrough and to do so they must use their actions wisely and the characters that frequent the great city. 

Notre Dame uses a draft, smaller in nature but much the same as that used in 7 Wonders. The draft gives each person 3 actions of which they can use 2 each round. Because all players have the same cards in their deck they must consider carefully which cards to keep and which to move on. Those paying attention can try to remember which cards other players have passed from their own decks and use that knowledge to make wise decisions. 

As the players improve their Burroughs they will also gain benefits for doing so. These in turn help to build an engine with which to earn Victory Points. 

One major consideration is that of the rat build up in the wharf district, which can lead to plague. At the end of each round (of which there are 9) each Burrough see an increase in rats. The players can try to keep this problem under control by improving their hospital and the park district to name but a few.

Notre Dame is a great game that plays in 40-50 minutes for experienced players and has plenty of options to ensure that not everything can be achieved. 

It also plays well with 2.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Just purchased, ~20 plays f2f and on BSW

Tense tight puzzle of a game. Plays in a short time and lets you know when you stuff up. Unforgiving to the unthinking, but not so much of a brain burner. Leaves you wanting to return for another go at beating your score. I don't think it is a multi-player solitaire. The passing of cards, carriage and playing to Notre Dame all change depending on what others are doing. People understanding this can really stifle your efforts or if you aren't thinking about this you can hand points to your opponents on a platter.

Plays very well with two.

RATING: 6.5
Notre Dame is a decent game in the Euro cube pusher genre. That may come off as a bit of a negative sounding statement but a lot of what people say about this game is correct. It is a little generic in its feel in that you move cubes around each turn and make decisions from a limited amount of possible actions. The game is certainly one of those games where you'd like to do A,B,C and D but only have enough resources to do A and B... and maybe sometimes C if you plan ahead well. The game rewards some careful planning as you try and balance your cash and number of cubes available while keeping a careful watch on the amount of detrimental effects (rats) that will count against you each round and lower your prestige point score. Let the rats build up too high and you lose cubes and prestige points. In the end all that matters IS prestige points so every action you decide on must take that into account... achieving as high a score as possible in the prestige point column is THE goal. 

The games strong suit is that there is a variable number of ways to achieve  that goal and no two games will play out quite the same and no two players will likely take the same path to achieving that goal either. There is luck  of the draw in terms of which cards you draw and which cards you are passed by other players each turn... but over the course of 9 rounds I feel the luck is reasonably dished out to all those involved. I like the card passing mechanism as there is often 2 or even 3 cards you want but you need to prioritize and make some hard choices to make sure your most needy area is dealt with first.

All in all this is a very mechanically sound game with a pretty solid "angst per minute" ratio. This is usually a sign of a solid game for me, yet Notre Dame just comes up a bit short so far. It seems as if the ideas are solid enough and the mechanisms are solid enough yet the sum of the parts doesn't quite add up to a magnificent, memorable game. Instead I think the game is solid and enjoyable played occasionally but not a game that I would want to play all that often. 

I give this game a 6.5 for now and admit that may be a bit low based on the gameplay which is interesting enough but not good enough to inspire repeat plays of any regularity and that's my main qualitative evaluation of a game... how much do I want to play it again and again. As we play a few more times my appreciation may rise for this game but there are other games both heavier and lighter in weight that I'd rather see on the table more often.

Ratings:
Initial(wow factor): 8 (a bit confusing looking and the board sections are initally hard to distinguish)
Family: 9 (everyone will play this one)
Game group: 7 (if time is short it gets played but not otherwise)
AP rating: 9 (little downtime)
Solo play: 9 (mimics the normal play well and plays very quick - #5 for SoloPlay currently)

When purchased: 2008
Recommended expansions: none available
Age range: 8+
Luck factor: 5

Reason purchased: Geek and gamer's recommendation through on-line play

Pros: plays quick, there are a mixture of light to moderate decisions to be made, the game scales well and solo play (it is multi-player solitaire) was an easy step
Cons: after many plays the best strategies are obvious, very little variability in games, many moves are very obvious especially in the last round

Recommendation: it is a good light game that if not played too often provides a nice break from reality.  Solo play is more interesting than klondike and has a similiar feel.  Serious players will probably not have long term interest in the mechanics nor the game.
Replay rating: Below average (heavy initially but it has tapered off drastically)
Game value (play/cost ratio):slightly above average due to its family friendly feel

RATING: 8
Game based on the Notre Dame Cathedral. Each player has a borough with 7 areas and their own deck of cards. Each round, they draw three cards, pick one and pass the other two to the left, choose another, so they end up with 3 cards total. Only 2 are played. Cards put tokens into the boroughs or Notre Dame, to gain VPs, money, more tokens, hospital, hotel, etc. At the end of each round, persons available for hire contribute to a plague total, which can be reduced. If not, VPs are lost if one exceeds the plague value of 9. Game ends after 9 rounds. This is a very good game, with many tough decisions to make. Each game is different, and there are many strategies to try, given that the cards come out in different orders and combinations. This is a well designed game.

RATING: 9
Fun game. I like the fact that you specialize in attributes; the more cubes you place there, the stronger you get in that particular area. And it plays very quickly, particularly online.

Notre Dame is a dense game, and difficult to grok. On the surface, it is a simple card drafting role-selection economic game. It requires a pretty deep understanding of how to build your engine and score points that does not come with the typical skim-the-surface experience most players have with the game. However, once you achieve basic mastery of the game (usually with at least a couple dozen plays, the basic underlying strategies begin to emerge and the possibility of strong player interaction becomes apparent. The game becomes better, the more you play and understand it.

UPDATE: Our group has had fun with this one, and even Wifey got in on the action. 9->9.5

Highest score I've seen is 93. New personal best is 85 using a combination of 4 in park, carriage, residence, and ND. woot!

RATING: 7

RATING: 8
還不錯的小品  
不過感覺人多點好像路線變化性比較多 
動作好像永遠都不夠
規則:
*根據人數設訂版圖，洗勻六張棕色朋友卡，9張灰色照背面英文字母洗勻並照ABC疊起
*每個玩家拿對應顏色物件，馬車放在最接近自己那塊版圖中央，四個影響力標記一個朋友token，三塊錢，信件版塊隨機放在版圖四個角落，鼠疫標記在0，起始玩家拿標記，個人牌庫九張牌洗勻蓋著
*遊戲進行ABC三個大輪，每大輪有三個小回合，每個小回合有五個階段
A 開兩張棕色一張灰色朋友卡
B 從個人牌庫抽三張牌，進行輪抽直到每個玩家手上都有三張
C 輪流打出第一張，之後輪流打出第二張(第三張不執行)打出的牌之後蓋著不可以看
D 付一塊錢 雇用一張朋友卡，沒有獨佔
E 調整鼠疫軌
*每回合結束把朋友卡開著放在各自牌堆下，起始玩家左傳，每大輪結束結算聖母院，根據影響力個數比例拿聖母院上的分，除時無條件捨去，方塊丟回中央供應區，洗勻棕色朋友卡，每個玩家拿回各自九張卡洗勻成為牌庫
*結束分數最高獲勝，平手比錢+影響力方塊總和
*九個行動如下
1.學校 放一個影響力標記在該區，該區有幾個拿幾個影響力方塊到自己供應區
2.銀行 放一個影響力標記在該區，該區有幾個拿幾塊錢
3.住宅 放一個影響力標記在該區，該區有幾個拿幾分
4.驛站 放一個影響力標記在該區，該區有幾個可移動馬車幾步(可以不用移滿)
收集郵件同顏色要拿第二個時必須其他顏色都先拿到，第三個同理，除非沒有了
5.旅館 三選一，四顆以上，三選二可重複
6.朋友 放朋友token到任意區，並執行該行動，一定要換位置，本身也算一個影響力
7.公園 退一格鼠疫，每兩個影響力標記，每次加分都額外加一分
8.醫院 退一格鼠疫，之後增加鼠疫格時要先減掉此格上的影響力個數
9.聖母院 付1/2/3元得1/3/6分並放一個影響力(放跟錢都要能做到)到聖母院
*如果個人供應區沒有影響力標記，執行動作時可改為從其他區(包括聖母院)移動到該卡的地區，也可棄牌不做事
*當鼠疫軌超過9時放在9，該玩家失去兩分，並從最多影響力的地區拿一個影響力標記放回公用供應區
*兩人遊戲使用四人圖版，信件會另外放兩個中立顏色
*該回合如果鼠疫總和為負數，會退鼠疫格

RATING: 8.5
One of Feld's early games that lead to his current status as a "victory point salad" designer. As always, there are too many ways to get points and not enough actions to feel like you're accomplishing anything great. Game play is gathered around a card-drafting mechanic of ten different cards every round; play two, but "burn" one. And because victory points are kept in front of you, not on a track where everyone can see who's leading, the winner isn't clear until the very last round. Any time I've played this game, I feel like I'm doing well and then realize I'm in last place; it's fun until it's not. What I most appreciate about his games is that there is always an anti-victory point action; an element that will eat at the points you've worked so hard for. It can be distracting because you might have to take time off from building your engine for victory points, but those strategies don't have to be exclusive to each other in a turn. Meaningful decisions are at the core of every Feld game; you won't know that you're eliminating your path to victory until much later in the game. The cards you draft from round to round may limit or free you; if you're the only one doing that one particular action, chances are you're winning. The best element of design for this game is that the board changes with the amount of players; and they resemble stained glass when put together. Not one of my favorites from this designer but definitely enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game fabulously suits my engine building and low-pus preferences. You want to do everything - get money to pay for an extra action each turn, get your rat protection up to avoid losing points each turn, get your cube count up so you can build the power of your chosen actions, and then, oh yeah, actually score points. But of course you can’t do it all. Your strategy is driven by the action cards you draw (there are 9 different actions), what action cards remain, the knowledge of which actions you pass away to your rivals (and thus won’t be able to do in a later turn) plus your analysis of what action cards your rivals will likely pass to you given their board state and previous passes to you. The action card passing means everyone’s strategies diverge, and the striving to get the most out of the strategy you settle on is engaging. It makes for a clever and thinky Euro, but without being over-thinky - it moves along at a nice pace. To do well, you need to know and plan for the end-of-game bonuses (maximising the benefits of as many as possible), and this is a potential downside for new players that may hinder their enjoyment, a hump that needs to be overcome. With replay though, I’ve found it provides me a new and interesting challenge each game that I thoroughly enjoy.

RATING: 6
"Quick, but feels long. I'm not a fan of pass-drafting in general, but dislike it even more here. More complicated than its fans think."

What I like:
- Modular board is an amazingly clever design
- Low confrontation, and no majority scoring
- Finished in 90 minutes with 5p and 3 first-timers
- Rat mechanism is nice

What I don't like:
- Pass-drafting
- Even at 90 minutes it felt long
- Drafting should alternate directions

It is more complicated than it's 2.8 weight rating. Mechanically there are several things going on, and the cards are special abilities that you have to first learn and memorize, and the during the game remember what you have seen and what you have not.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fantastic game! When you explain it to other players it sounds really complex, but once you start playing it, it's really incredibly easy to get the hang of. The box says it may take up to 75 mins, but if you know your way, this game can be played in little over 30 mins, 45 max! Even with 5 players!

The great thing is also that, no matter how many players are playing, it's the same challenge! Unlike most of the games that are for a variable number of players, in this game, players always play with the exact same resources, whereas in other games you got more components for less players, which makes thing "delute" a bit and doesn't mostly provide the pretended experience (think Puerto Rico for example), not the case here! Fantastic work Stefan Feld!!!

RATING: 9
Questo sì che è un gioco tedesco! Mezza pagina di regole, bella ambientazione (per essere un German Game), ottima scalabilità e longevità. L'interazione, a prima vista assente, è invece onnipresente: sia indiretta (la rotazione delle carte azione al tuo vicino) che diretta (la conquista delle tessere con la carrozza, la competizione per Notre Dame). Le meccaniche di gioco, elegantissime, lo fanno filar liscio fino alla sua conclusione: se tutti lo conoscono già, si può arrivare a terminare il gioco in meno di un'ora. Una spruzzata di alea la danno, oltre alle carte azione, sia le tessere che i personaggi a fine turno. Questi ultimi, oltre a introdurre un po' di varietà, hanno un ruolo a volte fondamentale nel bilancio di un Quartiere o nella realizzazione di punti prestigio. Da segnalare la plancia di gioco "mutante" a seconda del numero di giocatori: una lezione di geometria. Difetti: come in altri giochi - mi viene in mente Puerto Rico - la presenza di un giocatore esperto alla tua destra può renderti la vita difficile. Inoltre, la qualità di alcuni materiali - specialmente i "petali" - non è eccelsa. 
Un gioiello.

At first Notre Dame was a rather bleach experience. I thought it was pretty mediocre, pleasant enough but lacking in personality. I would probably have rated it a "6" if I had rated it after the first play. But over time, and with more plays, my impressions have changed and continue to do so - this is indeed a terrific little game. 

I think this is the game that made me change my opinion the most over time in a positive way (Pillars of the Earth is the game that change the most with more plays in a negative way). 

It is somewhat of a "multiplayer solitaire", and it plays equally good with 2,3 or 4 players (5 is not that good). Analytical and a bit on the dry side, but with extremly good replayability. The game is not that hard to grasp, but there is a obvious learning curve, so with more plays you'll do better and better. Me and my wife plays this often.

RATING: 10
I liked it when I first played it. After 5 plays I thought it was very good. After 40 plays both online 
and face to face, I think it's great! It aged really well.

The game is very clever: the choices are constrained by the game itself (the available districts), by luck (the cards that are drawn) and by the other players (the cards that are passed around). Players need to have a long term strategy but also adapt constantly to the situation. There's a definite learning curve, because a key element in the game is planning ahead for specific characters.

It's also a pretty fast affair and scales very well from 2 to 5. Player interaction is low and subtle, but that is actually a plus for me.

I am swept away by Feld's lighter titles! This is my third Feld that I've played(second that I own) and I am loving it!

I am more of a family games' guy so this fits my taste better than Trajan (2nd Feld I've played). This game has the rat mechanics to make you feel that you have "tough" decisions to make throughout the game.

RATING: 5
Initially I started buying a couple of the titles in the Alea range due to rave reviews, but I soon realised by #4 (Princes or Puerto Rico?) that I am not the target audience. And now playing #11 (this one) reminds me again why the word Alea doesn't excite me. Interesting and unique card play in that you have to pass 2 of the 3 cards drawn from your own deck; there is some inherent fun in this as there can be coincidences of being given a card you have just passed on as well as giving a useless card. Played with 3 a couple of times which I think is the sweet spot because you know something of what will be in your 2 competitor's hands. A recent (and a long time between another) play with 5 has forced a rating drop due to having to relearn what the sectors and cards do, so less of a game and more of a question and answer session. This experience highlights that although ND is an interesting management game (with the pressure of rats ruining your status) it is the typical "many cost effective avenues to vps exercise" and so has to wait for it's turn in the sun.

RATING: 6

That said, it's an enjoyable game (I played it 590 times after all) and I would not kill your pleasure by revealing any strategy tips and I recommend to nobody to look for them.

RATING: 8
P: 2-5, T: 60 min
Pick and pass roll selection, otherwise a bit of multi-player solitaire (low interaction). Minimal player downtime and luck outcomes (paying attention to your neighbors is still important). Clever modular board.  Light enough mechanics to teach easily, but several hidden layers of strategy (multiple paths to victory). Plays well with all player counts. 
Theme is implemented well; everyone we've introduced it to has enjoyed the different mechanics (containing the rats, carriage, cathedral). 

It really is a 'by-the-book' light Euro, it seems to do all the traditional things without over staying its welcome. Certainly a good stepping stone to other Euros. Very solid and satisfying game.

-One of the districts feels a bit under powered (can't say I've even seen the Inn as anything but a last resort). There is a well regarded expansion which generates additional variety game to game that is largely unavailable =(
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a really interesting engine building and drafting game with a ton of player interaction. The idea is that each player gets a symmetrical deck of cards. On their turn they draw three cards from their deck, keep 1 and pass the others, until everyone has a three card hand again. Then each player gets to resolve two of the three cards as actions. The actions build on each other, so all following times you preform action A, Action A gets stronger in it's effect. You do this three times, and at the end of each of these there is a scoring of the center tile.

The game has an interesting system where if you don't take actions to prevent rats, then you wind up taking penalties. It's curiously a viable strategy to both invest into rat prevention and ignore rats completely and focus on grabbing as many points as possible and just eating the penalties. Since each player only gets one card per age that allows them to place a cube into the center scoring area, these cards tend to not get passed, which can make the center scoring a little anti-climactic.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:nostar: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
I love the multiple paths to victory in Notre Dame.  I also enjoy simultaneous role selection and Notre Dame throws a twist on this mechanic by using drafting in conjunction with simultaneous selection.  I also love the two-fold role selection aspect.  In the first part of the turn, the drafting part, the other players cannot usually take the actions you take (when you take an action it limits the other players ability to take this action).  The end part of the turn where you buy the assistance of a person, can be taken by everyone, but because each person has different options in the beginning of each turn, there are different advantages to take different assistants. I love the interaction of the two parts of each sub-turn.

While I think Notre Dame is great for two and three players, I don't like it as well for more because there will be one player who you can only affect with your carriage.  I love the interaction with the drafting mechanic and it does not work as well with more than three.  There is also no way to guess what cards the player(s) whose cards I never see have left.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
A good game that I enjoyed, but coming one week after a play of Trajan, knocking the rating down as I do prefer Trajan over this one.  I feel the game is a bit too limited in its actions available to players who are constantly trying to optimize the that they have.  A poor card draw on a turn can hamper when none are really good options and one must rely on the pass.  I did enjoy the challenge of trying to make the best of my limited hand of cards often changing my plans based on what I was passed, but that just made the game feel very tactical as one continued on the initial choices for the overall strategy in the game.  (A convoluted way of saying that it appears players will focus on a few districts as their overall strategy and then work them tactically as the game progresses.)  Trajan felt a bit more open-ended and the mancala mechanism left the player more in control of action choices.

RATING: 7.5
22-02-2015. Rated after 1 play. Notre Dame is the first Feld game I genuinely enjoyed a lot.

Each round 3 cards are available that you can pay 1 gold to get a special ability and which also show an amount of rats (more on these later). The players then draw three cards, they keep one and pass the two others, and all other players do the same. Players then play the cards in subsequent 'turns', all cards 'activate' one of the actions on the board, and you may put a cube on them from your storage, or move one from another action space you put cubes on previously. You then may take the action of a strength equal to the number of cubes on that space. The spaces allow you to get cubes from the supply, get money, get VPs, drive a carriage around to get chips with VPs/or abilities. 

The last two actions protect against rats, slowing rats down or reducing the number of rats you have had in total. If you ever reach the end of the 'rattrack' on the board (9 rats), you lose 2 VPs and one of your cubes at the highest location dies.

I really, really like the game. It's drafting with a focus (what do I want to get this round) and penalty (rats). You build an engine, which I like, to make the most VPs (can't really care for those). But I, really, really liked this game. Are their downsides, I didn't like the artwork which was a bit bland.. nor the central focus of the notre dame, as it doesn't really impact the game as much as you might think.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I only rate this one high because of the fact that it has drafting, predating the big growth of this mechanic in 7 Wonders and Sushi Go. But the action drafting is just not that interesting (and one action - the "take 1 gold/1 cube/kill rat" - just always seems sub-optimal compared to other options).  It's just a bit too dry for me, lacking much fun factor, with little in the way of player interaction or multiple victory paths.

RATING: 6
Interesting and solid but not exceptional game in the Luis XIV (which I consider to be a very average, abstract game) mold: trade certain resources for other resources. Action selection is a bit like Citadels (pick one, give the others to the left).

However, there seem to be issues. Like Puerto Rico, this is a screw your left hand neighbour, and hope your right doesn't screw you.
And the random cards can sometimes really hurt you, when two cards you need come up at the same time (of which you may only keep one).

Notched a further point for rather poor component quality: my main boards warp, and I've got 10  tokens with ripped edges or split card board.

RATING: 9
Turns out that this is a hidden gem of a game. A lot of tense decisions through out the whole of game.

Do you let the rat plague breach and lose prestige points and game board influence, but possible get more prestige points in another area or do I try and mitigate the plague?

How do I get more influence cubes so I can decrease rat plague by influencing the Hospital or Park areas?

Do I have enough money so I can go to Notre Dame for extra prestige points, or as bribe for my 3'rd card action?

Lots of decisions like these, a well crafted game with a unique modular game board that morphs based upon 3, 4, 5 players.

RATING: 7.5
First impression.  Very good solid Euro.  A _little_ solitairey I guess - but competing for shared points and most importantly the card drafting (which is v important and also very cool) keeps some player interaction in the game so not purely a race against the others.

Looks like there are plenty of routes to take to victory and plenty of choices to be made along the way which is always good.  Play time should be down to about 1.25 - 1.5hrs after a couple of plays I reckon.  Being swamped by plagues of rats and trying to fight off the infestation is hilarious.

The player mats/board are very neat indeed.  Probably nothing too earth shattering mechanics-wise, but they all seemed to fit together seamlessly to give a polished result.  Overall, I guess I wasn't super duper excited by it, but I did still enjoy it quite a lot.  Worth a look.

Has gone up in my rankings after a few more plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I'm fairly disappointed in Notre Dame. I expected to like it very much, it sounded like just my kind of game. After my first play I was very frustrated with the action selection mechanism, as it's difficult to do anything that you really want to do on purpose. The draft mechanic ties you into your right hand opponent (and his right hand opponent) in such a way that if they want to do the same thing you want to do, then you're pretty much out of luck - you need to switch your strategy. Of course you can't know that's the case until you're 1/3 of the way through the game! Because you always pass left, the game encourages people to settle into a strategy that's not disrupted by the players to their right, and stick to it. There's little means by which to hinder any other player (defensive draft) because you only draft 3 cards at a time, and you can't really afford to tank your own play to keep someone else from having a good action.

I feel like this game would be better if the draft changed directions each round, or if you drafted more cards at a time, or if instead of drafting at all you played the cards like Kreta, one at a time from the whole supply... something.

Edit: Played again, it seems better, but I'm still annoyed by parts of it. I could see liking this game now, but I don't yet.

Edit again: I've played again, and I've started to "get it." I like the game now :)

Edit a third time: I played some 2p tonight, and I don't like it much, mostly because when you draw the Notre Dame card you almost have to draft it, even if you want the other cards. Unless your opponent is neglecting income, you are handing them tons of points if you don't.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Notre Dame is a draft-driven game which distills the typical Feld experience into a brisk hour. Players are given individual city quarters to manage, and gradually increase the efficiency of actions by investing influence in them. Most elements in the game – resource juggling, multiple VP sources and rat penalties – will be familiar territory for contemporary gamers, although I'd like to think that this game set the tone for Feld's later designs.

The board is rather interesting too, and its unique shape actually plays a minor role in the game. Notre Dame is neither deep nor innovative, but the relative simplicity and snappy playing time makes its quite an attractive title.

RATING: 7.5
Very playable eurogame that helpe restore some respectability to the Alea line (after the dud that was Rum and Pirates). This time, players are drafting cards in order to activate various sectors on the board. Each sector offers either VPs, money and other ways to score influence. There are a fair few things to manage but the decision tree is small (you are essentially only drafting two cards to play each turn). Still, there are agonising moments and the whole package clips along at a good pace. Probably worth a few plays before it gets stale.

RATING: 9
An amazingly done card drafting game with area activation. Gather as much Prestige as possible by sending your workers to parks, banks or the titular cathedral, all the while avoiding the plague and bribing nobles for bonuses. The game lets you get ahead on points early in the game or slowly bid your time and gather resources to score massive points in the second half of the game, which is pretty much the staple of Stefan Feld's games. Overall, I wish I could play this more often as it scales fantastically well to any number of players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is a highly tactical, build-your-machine, risk management system.  That said, I like those kinds of games.  If you want any significant strategy I'd say look elsewhere as your game play is completely dictated by the cards.  I tend to like games that you can play by the seat of your pants.  This game is really all about doing the best you can under the conditions you are given, I'd imagine that this could be really frustrating if you were expecting anything more. 

It was readily apparent at the beginning of the game that you were going to be thrown to the wind and with this it feels like you are continually facing little challenges, to keep the ship on course.  

Sadly, the only interaction is the carriage race and the Magic: The Gathering/Fairy Tale card draft. It seems that people are really going crazy with the card passing mechanic this year, this is yet another variation on the theme, which is a more controlled form of drafting.  Still I prefer this 2006/7 take on drafting to the old Venture mechanic that has now been beaten into submission (See: Ticket to Ride/Thurn & Taxis/Pillars of the Earth).

I'm a little bummed that there is no Quasimodo.

UPDATE: Downgraded this to a 7. The more I play this the more annoyed I am with the endgame.  You are forced throughout the game to fashion your "strategy" on what cards you are given, but when you get to the endgame there is often nothing worthwhile that you can do.  It feel anti-climactic to me, and frankly it is irritating.  I still like the game, but it just feels like the plug is pulled.  I wish there were other points to award, even if it were a pittance at the end of the game so that there was something useful you could do with useless cards.

With three players there is more intention to the cards you are passing as you can effect every player, with four your cards only effect the next two downstream.  I prefer the three player game.  Of course, the carriage competition increases with more players, a toss up?

UPDATE: I am compelled to return to this game. While it may not be perfect, I find that it is a joy to play.  Who knows, the rating may start climbing higher again...

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It's a economic resource management type game with hand management and set-collection stuff going on, too.

The board is very cool, designed to be put together for 2-5 players. Each player is largely playing in their own area, although they also have a wagon that can move around through all areas collecting tokens; and the central area (Notre Dame) is accessible to all. Play is driven by the cards and cubes. There are 9 areas corresponding to the 7 sectors of each player's area, 1 to Notre Dame, and 1 to move their Friend token. You get cards by drafting 3 at a time: draw 3 each round (3 rounds per period; score at end of period); keep 1 and pass the remainder to the left; repeat. Then, you'll play 2 of the 3 (in turn order). Cards generally allow you to place your cube into the corresponding sector. When you do so, you get the benefit (e.g., get gold, get VP, reduce #rats, etc.) of the sector; these generally improve as the #cubes there increases. After 2 cards played, can pay 1 gold to hire 1 person (cards set up; different 3 cards each period) who gives you some benefit. Then, resolve rats: these last cards have 0-4 rats on them; everyone adds the total # of rats to their count. If they get above 9, they lose 2 VP and 1 cube. Also score Notre Dame: each cube their gets VP = 12/(total # cubes there).

Repeat all this for total of 9 turns. Most VP at end wins!

There's a lot going on but the game plays quite quickly (69 min for 5 newbies). There are multiple paths to victory, depending on how you want to allocate your cubes. There may be some problems with luck of the card draw, but you will eventually have a choice of 3 from all your own cards, as well as what's passed to you, so the randomness is mostly manageable. Plays especially well with 3, but really works with any number. Definitely worth having!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
On a first play, I had little idea about what I should do. This game sounded pretty simple from the rules explanation, but being my first game of this type ever played, it felt a bit overwhelming. There are lots of things happening in the game. I liked the idea of having own "district" to expand and a place to put up some competition (Notre Dame).
The board looks awesome no matter how many players play. The tiles look great when put together with varying centre piece. The drafting of action cards was a bit confusing at the beginning, choosing the right card so that you won't help too much your oponents is a tricky thing.
This game requires lots of plays to really get into, but it's really nice piece with some interesting strategies.

RATING: 7
Stefan Feld is one of my favorite designers.  I like so many of his games because of their incredibly complex and clever mechanics even though they are typically quite light on theme.  This one is no exception.

In this game each player is controlling a burrough surrounding the Notre Dame cathedral.  Each player has a deck of 9 cards and at the start of each round they draw 3 cards, pick one and pass the other two.  Each player will end up with 3 cards, one of each player's color, and play 2 of those.  Each represents an action from collecting money, to points, to managing the rat population in your burrough.  After each player has taken 2 actions they can purchase the ability of one of 3 person cards and then they have to increase the rat population in their burrough.  If the rat population gets too high they lose points and influence (action) cubes in an area.

The game lasts until the players have gone through their deck 3 times, so nine rounds.  The rounds are incredibly quick making this a very quick game.  Probably about an hour with the full complement of 5 and under with fewer players.  I love the clever interaction of giving away your action cards and planning your turns around your neighbor's leftovers.  And the rules are straightforward and the rounds quick enough to make this a very accessible game.

It isn't his most stellar design (I had high expectations since this is the game credited most with Feld's fame) but it is still good and a game I enjoy playing.

RATING: 4
I was moved to rate this by a review describing the game as "not just another cube pusher".

What?!?  It is EXACTLY just another cube pusher.  You have 27 actions, and each of them involves choosing to do one of a set of proscribed things that convert thingies into thingies.  At the end of the game, whoever has the most victory thingies wins.  The theme is wafer-thin, and the only semi-interesting parts of the game are the rats and the carriages.  The core of the game's uniqueness, the MTG-like "drafting" of roles, is pretty silly, with certain cards almost always dominating others and so the hope is that you don't get too many of the powerful cards in one hand and your neighbor to the right does.  Woo hoo.

The one good thing the game does, and what may lure me back to it at some point, is that it is short enough that the classic "build your engine, then run your engine" strategy isn't necessarily the ideal one.  Jam today can be a reasonable approach.  So I give it marks for not being easily decipherable, but the actual game mechanics are pretty dreary with no real theme to hang them on.  I've never seen a game more in need of a "space empire" re-theming.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another worker placement Euro game of very high quality, which it has to be to stand out in an overcrowded genre. It's set in 14th Century Paris where you play the head of a family controlling a borough made up of seven areas. Each area brings bonuses to you by placing influence within them e.g the bank brings you money, the hospital cures the effects of the plague, the residence allows you to increase the amount of influence available to you etc. The goal is to accumulate the highest amount of prestige (victory points), which is determined by where you apportion your influence. Excellent balance of play leaves you having to make full use of every decision you're posed with and you cannot waste a single move. One of the very best games of the worker placement genre.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Dang thing's been sittin' on my shelf for 2 years now, maybe more.  I played a 2 player game of it twice.

It's a medium euro with very straight-forward rules, smooth flow and low-level analysis paralysis--for those prone to it.  I love the Feld avoided AP by only really providing about 3 choices at all times.

It gets a 9 and not a 10 from me because, it feels a bit dated and theme (as usual for most euros) doesn't come through strongly, but it's not bad.  The rat scale, central cathedral, Quazi modo marker and the carriage piece give it some nice thematic elements.  

If my wife can get over horrifying difference in our scores from the first play, I would love to try this again with her--or friends... Or anyone.

RATING: 8
A Feld game I like! I can see the future Feld in Notre Dame, but he hadn't yet graduated to the "point salad" school of design. Notre Dame does contain multiple ways to score points, but the game, overall, is much less fiddly and allows for inter-player interaction through the carriages and card draft. The rat/plague mechanic was enjoyable. The threat of point and worker loss added to the already tight game economy for an overall tense experience. There is still the feel of multi-player solitaire, with private point optimization being the primary focus of the game; however, the game was streamlined enough to make it an enjoyable experience.

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 2
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 2
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 2

First Impression:

A quick strategy game, that offers a variety of strategies, enough replayability and little luck.

I love the flow of the game, as it plays fast but every decision you have to make is crucial and most are also hard. In addition the draft mechanic in combination with a couple directly interactive actions make this game more interactive than most euro games.

Some actions appear poorer than others, but I'm not really sure about this yet. The theme is working better than most classic euros, but it's still not enough for me.

Overall, I like this game, and I expect that my rating will rise the more I play.

RATING: 7
As a Feld fan, I've been a bit remiss in not trying this game out. In my defense, it came out a few years before I got into the hobby and it's out of print. I finally got the chance to play it, and...

This is a really clever game. I really liked it a lot. Like most Feld games the neat mechanics are what really make the game. Every player has a personal deck of nine cards. You draw three, keep one and pass the remainder, drafting your opponent's cards. You will end up with a card from the ones you drew and one from each of your two neighbors to the right. And you can play two of the three cards you get. There are three rounds in each phase, and three rounds in each phase. So you'll see all the cards in your deck three times, which means you'll never be completely blocked out of an action each phase.

Playing cards involves either placing a cube from your personal supply on your board, or moving a cube around on your board (the former is usually preferable). Coins are largely used to hire one of the three employee cards that are available each round. They do various cool things but they also bring rats with them. The game is largely about avoiding getting too many rats, while getting cubes, coins and VPs.

This is a game of tough choices. You'll always want to do more than you can, and choosing what you need most can be agonizing! The onslaught of rats can be brutal, so you'd best be prepared. The random employees that come out and the random draw/draft mean that every game is going to be different.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The players take on the roles of the heads of influential families in Paris at the end of the 14th century. In the shadow of the Notre Dame cathedral, the players compete for prosperity and reputation. Each family controls one of the 3 -5 boroughs that surround the site of Notre Dame. As head of his family, each player tries, through clever use of his action cards, to advance the power and prestige of his family, but penalties are assessed those who do not take care of the health of the people who live in their borough. The player with the most prestige at the end is the winner.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
08/2010:6.5
An Alea game means a higher bar to pass. I think the game is not bad. The mechanics do seem a little hodge-podge and a mish-mash of area control as well as role selection. The only unique aspect of the game is the card selection followed by passing to the left rule. You generally will know the cards you can pick, but won't know which cards you will get from your neighbour. Also, you can play only 2 cards per round. That prevents you from having to play a "bad" card and hence wasting resources.
One other good thing of note is the lack of downtime. This game can flow pretty fast with few decisions to make each turn.
Not the best Feld design. ITYOTD is a lot better. I don't hate this game, nor am I terribly fond of it. That is why, I rate it a 6.5.

RATING: 8
To an extent, it is very much your generic manage-your-own-fiefdom Eurogame- I was particularly struck by how the the odd shape of the districts, ingeniously devised to fit together for 2, 3, 4, or 5 players, was essentially a way of covering for the fact that these were essentially separate player mats.

That being said, I don't have anything against generic manage-your-own-fiefdom Eurogames, especially if they have enough subtlety, enough little gears and levers such that figuring the game out is enjoyable.  And it is with Notre Dame, and I expect will be so for awhile yet.

RATING: 10
First play - 5 players. What a surprise this game was! I thought it was going to be a dry Euro game but what I got was a Euro game with card drafting and rats! As with the best Euro games there are multiple choices each turn and many paths to victory. You can't be everywhere at once but if you have a solid strategy and stick with it you can score well. The owner of the game blew us away as he never even got overrun by rats as the rest of us did at least once. I must have been doing something right because I finished second and I know I made several misplays. The random placement of the tokens on the map which you can drive to and collect and the three "hire" cards that come up each turn give the game a nice touch of randomness that I appreciate. The hires also bring the rats. Maybe ther will only be 1 rat this turn but maybe there will be 9!!! If yo udon't play for it they will hurt you. The end game was a bit of a mad rush to score victory points and if you planned well you can leave your opponents in the dust.

RATING: 5
I love games where scoring VP points represents something. Building a city, perhaps. Conquering Europe, maybe. 

In Notre Dame, you spend lots of time fiddling with a puzzle in order to... score maximum VP points. The puzzle doesn't represent anything thematically meaningful - it is just a point-scoring exercise. As you get good at the game, your opponents will try to stop you scoring more VP points by devious drafting... or maybe moving their carriage into your space or something. Wahahahah! 

I've played the game three times, won three times, and I can't face playing it a fourth time. Yes, I may discover how to optimise the elusive 'park' strategy, but I could be doing something equally satisfying and meaningful for 30 minutes. Like taking my socks on-and-off fifteen times.

RATING: 7
Our second games was 'Notre Dame', which I recall playing before where players try to establish the biggest presence in their district of Paris by acquiring prestige points, money, hospitals etc. all this whilst trying to prevent rat infestations within the city limits. I must confess I did not grasp the manner by which the rats were reduced so after the first turn, I was losing two victory points from that, and believed that hospitals reduced this every turn for each influence cube allocated? I felt a little hard done by as all the rat catchers were bought up before it was my turn as the player turn order didn't fall into place for me. I did at least secure one of every colour token from each player district. Pete H won as none of us put any influence in Notre Dame after the first turn so ten points straight away. I think the three of us missed a trick here as from then on in, Notre Dame was always contested, so no clean sweep. The bidding system makes this a very tactical game, which I like, so I will certainly play again and perhaps learn from my (significant) mistakes. Who put the rats out?

RATING: N/A
Players play as well-off Parisians in the 15th century who wish to improve the importance and appearance of the city quarter around the famous Notre Dame cathedral. The primary game concept is original but simple card play which players use to permanently improve their influence in the quarter. However, turn after turn, round after round, players must make choices that can have major implications. Because if one does one thing, then the other can't be done any more. Concentrating on one aspect means automatically ignoring another. Which, above all others, is particularly dangerous in the case of the gradually approaching plague...

Players play as well-off Parisians in the 15th century who wish to improve the importance and appearance of the city quarter around the famous Notre Dame cathedral. The primary game concept is original but simple card play players use to permanently improve their influence in the quarter. However, turn after turn, round after round, players must make choices that can have major implications. If one does one thing, then the other can't be done. Concentrating on one aspect means automatically ignoring another, which, above all others, is particularly dangerous in the case of the gradually approaching plague....
\13\\13\
After 9 exciting rounds and about 75 action-filled minutes, Notre Dame is over. The 'maitre' who has made the most of his cards and has garnered the most prestige points is the winner.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like the tension between the desire to specialize and the need to diversify.  The card passing is a good touch (the game would work without it, which is interesting and odd, but it's clearly much better with it).  The plague is a nasty albatross once it starts, but in a fun way. I also very much like the pace and flow.

It's also rather interesting in that it avoids being overbalanced; you can fall behind with no hope through seemingly reasonable play.  Not actually reasonable, of course, but it's nice to see a euro without explicit balancing (cf. Rudiger Dorn, who I like, but who tends toward overbalance), where you're working without a net.  Better tension because of this (though it's going to punish newbies more than usual, a necessary tradeoff).

Does feel a bit samey, though, after ~10 plays (I've played it 20+ times). The Treasure Chest expansion helps address this, and I will never play without it.

RATING: 8.5
Played several times face-to-face and on BSW (with 2 to 5 players). An interesting game, with quite a lot of tactical decisions. The randomness in the card draw does not bother me too much (knowing how to adapt to the cards which you draw and which get passed to you really is the key), nor does the possible unbalance between the various effects. The cleverness of the adjustable game board has to be noted - even though it is nothing else than a curious gimmick. After having tried the game with all the possible numbers of players, I think I prefer it with 3 (but 2 is very good as well, and the game remains enjoyable, though less easy to control, with more).

RATING: 7
{ P - [2] 3 4 5 }

[b]Preference in a Vacuum[/b]
Bank/Hospital > Park/Cube > Carriage/Advisor > Else

[b]Minstrel[/b]
More often than not this is how you get four cubes on Park. Look for him during A and B rounds. There's an outside chance to deposit two from Carriage, Cube, or Hotel to Hospital in A1-A2.

[b]Slowplay Advisor[/b]
Advisor is like a wild - free cube, free card. Once used however, he's not quite wild (can't repeat action) and he's his own cube. If he's stranded on Park, Hospital, Bank, or Cube he's effectively a dead piece, depending on your plans, so be careful.

[b]Bank[/b]
Bank is arguably better than Hospital because the point differential tends to be greater when you withhold Bank than Hospital from your opponent. Moreover, as with the Park you can Minstrel cubes to the Hospital because the incremental benefit is negligible, as opposed to Bank where you need each activation. +10 gold is a good target; know if/how it's going to happen before the C round.

[b]Extra Cubes Are Overrated[/b]
4 starting supply + 7/10 stock is enough for 4-Park, 3 Hospital, 3 Bank/Carriage with an extra to spare, plus Advisor. The extra cubes offer flexibility - and Monk is often the best Hiree present - but know what you need to make three groups of three for the C-round and what's superfluous.

[b]Hirees = Bank/Hospital[/b]
Barring Minstrel and high point Beggar King / Guild Master, try to focus your hirees on Bank and Hospital. Jester a random cube to Bank/Hospital, look for early A-round Bishop. Moneylender (and then some) is an acceptable substitute for Bank #4, Monk is your saving grace for passing Cube in favor of Bank/Hospital.

[b]BSW - The Best Game of Notre Dame Never Played[/b]

RATING: 5
3 Plays on BSW, 1 F2F. At first impressed with the mechanics and inter-connectedness of the game, despite the coaches and drafting being the only true level of player interaction. But the more I played, the less interesting the game became. Based on the very discrete utility of the locations and easily evaluated worth of action cards and bribed personage cards, it seems that with more play, your turns and actions may be "dictated" by the available cards, leaving little room for differentiation. The faceted game board pieces are a marvel - rearranging them to adjust the space for multiple players is elegant and incredibly creative. I have reservations about the game's long term or F2F appeal... the BSW implementation is excellent, and takes all of 15-25 minutes, the F2F game seems to crawl along at a snail's pace.

RATING: 7.5
Playing Notre Dame is kind of like juggling.  The delicate balance between influence cubes, money, rats and VP has to be constantly observed.  The randomness from the card drafting means you can't really plan ahead and must make the hard choices depending on the boroughs you get.  Same with the person cards, while they provide a kind of quick relief if you happen to run short on cubes or coins, or is in desperate need of an additional rat exterminated, but the VP opportunities they offer are often too important to be overlooked.

After playing a couple of times on BSW and seeing scores in the high 80's.  It seems that although there are multiple paths to victory, not all of them are equal and there is definitely one or two combination that will result in such a crushing win.  I'm not sure if that's a design flaw or not, and whether it will dampen my enjoyment for the game is still uncertain.
